==PAGE:1==
 
 
Studying Bacillus Subtilis root colonization of 
different plant species 
Distinct morphotypes of B. subtilis coexisting in evolved population after root colonization. 
 
Master Thesis in Microbiology 
By Christopher Blake (s192066, wmx519) 
 
Supervisor:   
Prof. Ákos T. Kovács 
Co-supervisor: 
PhD-student Mathilde Nordgaard 
 
Submitted on: 10th of August 2020
==PAGE:2==
i 
 
Abstract  
The soil bacterium Bacillus subtilis is known to suppress pathogens as well as promote plant growth. 
It has therefore a huge potential as natural fertilizer, replacing chemicals traditionally used in 
agriculture, which are a known cause of environmental threats. Even though the potential of B. subtilis 
seems immense under laboratory conditions and in greenhouse trials, its success varies greatly when 
applied in fields. In order to fully exploit this potential, a better understanding of the interactions 
between B. subtilis and plants is urgently needed. The requirements for successful root colonization 
are of particular interest, as these are decisive for the beneficial relationship.   
In this study, B. subtilis was examined for root colonization through experimental evolution on two 
different plant species. Accordingly, four evolution experiments, one on Arabidopsis thaliana, one on 
Tomato, and two alternating between both plant species, were conducted by initially inoculating plant 
seedlings with B. subtilis and continuously transferring the newly colonized root to a new sterile root. 
Therefore, only bacterial cells successful in colonization continued in the experiment, allowing for a 
selection of a regular cycle of chemotaxis, biofilm formation and dispersal.  
The populations evolved rapidly, improved in root colonization and diversified into distinct 
morphotypes, including a potential biofilm matrix overproducing and a non-producing sub-
population. In addition to the appearance of distinct morphotypes, evolved isolates showed different 
biofilm capacity, motility and growth in response to plant polysaccharides, indicating distinct 
adaptation strategies.  
Interestingly, the evolved population as a whole seems to benefit from the diversification, as shown 
by improved root colonization by a mixed community of all three different morphotypes compared to 
colonization by each morphotype alone. Intriguingly, the non-producing isolate exploits the others 
and shows significantly improved colonization potential at the expense of co-colonizing overproducing 
isolates in mixed population.  
Finally, even though isolates evolved on distinct plant species were not able to outcompete each other 
on the plant species they evolved on, most of the isolates evolved on A. thaliana showed improved 
root colonization only on A. thaliana in monoculture, but not on Tomato, suggesting plant species-
specific adaptation paths of the bacterium.  
This thesis presents a first glimpse into experimental evolution of the plant growth-promoting 
rhizobacterium B. subtilis on plant roots. Our results especially highlight genetic diversification as a 
stable strategy to increase plant colonization.

Preface and Acknowledgements 
This Master Thesis represents the final part of my master degree in Biology-Microbiology at the 
University of Copenhagen. It results of my theoretical and experimental work conducted between 
August 2019 and August 2020 in Prof. Ákos Kovacs lab, Bacterial Interactions and Evolution, at the 
Department of Biotechnology at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU). This thesis focuses on 
experimental evolution of B. subtilis in root colonization on Arabidopsis thaliana and Tomato plants. 
Due to the lockdown caused by covid-19, a part of this project was shifted towards a more theoretical 
approach, resulting in a review summarizing the molecular aspects of plant growth promotion and 
protection by Bacillus subtilis (see Supplementary, 9.1.). This review is currently in preparation for 
submission to a scientific journal.  
 
First of all, I would very much like to thank my co-supervisor Mathilde for all the effort and work you 
put into helping me with my thesis. Thank you so much for always being there for me and supporting 
me during every phase of my thesis. Your dedication and enthusiasm are really admirable.  
I would like to thank especially my supervisor Ákos Kovács for all your guidance. Thank you for this 
great project and presenting so many opportunities to me. I am really grateful for your support during 
these uncertain times. 
Finally, I would like to thank all the other wonderful people from this group, especially Yicen, Anna, 
Carlos, Heiko and Xinli. I believe, I have asked all of you a few stupid questions at one point and you 
were always so helpful to me. Thank you all for a great year in this lab.  
It was a real pleasure to work with all of you. 
 
 
 
This thesis was submitted on the 10th of August 2020 by:

 List of figures 
Fig. 1: Scheme showing the beneficial effects of PGPR on their host plant. PGBR can benefit their 
host plant either through promoting crop yield, e.g. through producing phytohormones, increasing 
nutrient availability or reducing water stress, as well as through protecting the plants against 
pathogens, e.g. through direct competition or the production and secretion of secondary metabolites 
with antimicrobial properties. ................................................................................................................ 2 
Fig. 2: Simplified scheme of the chemotactic response in B. subtilis towards plant root exudates. A) 
The binding of an attractant by the chemotactic receptor CheV/CheW leads to the activation of the 
CheA kinase, which in turn phosphorylates CheY. CheY-P interacts with the motor by changing the 
direction of its rotation to counter-clockwise, leading to a run. B) Chemotaxis can be described as a 
biased random walk. This means bacteria are able to detect a gradient of an attractant, in this case, a 
root exudate (yellow star), and in response change the frequencies of runs, i.e. more runs towards the 
gradient and fewer runs away from the gradient. ................................................................................. 3 
Fig. 3: Schematic presentation showing the cycle of biofilm formation of B. subtilis on plant roots. 
During the initiation of biofilm formation on a plant root, motile cells with flagella attach and 
differentiate into non-motile matrix-producing cells, which start to form a monolayer of cells sticking 
together surrounded by extracellular matrix. Cells grow further and produce more matrix to form 
microcolonies, which eventually develop into a mature biofilm. During maturation cells inside the 
biofilm differentiate into distinct cell types including sporulating cells, competent cells and motile 
cells. Upon environmental, e.g. change in nutrient levels, or internal cues, e.g. quorum sensing, cells 
start to actively leave the matrix and the biofilm disperses. .................................................................. 4 
Fig. 4: Simplified scheme of the switch between motility and matrix production in B. subtilis under 
the control of Spo0A. Plant signals are recognized by the sensor kinases KinC and KinD, initiating a 
phosphorylation cascade to activate Spo0A~P. Upon phosphorylation, Spo0A~P induces the 
production of SinI and thereby represses SinR and derepression of slrR. This results in high levels of 
SlrR, which bind to SinR, derepressing matrix genes, while at the same time the formed SlrR-SinR 
complex represses motility genes. In addition, Spo0A~P represses AbrB and therefore relieves matrix 
genes. The Spo0A pathway is further regulated through the quorum sensing system, including the 
autoinducer Phr and the rap phosphatases, which negatively regulate the phosphorylation cascade of 
Spo0A. The autoinducer Phr is translated as Pre-Phr proteins, which are secreted and processed into 
mature Phr peptides. At high cell density, Phr will reach a threshold concentration allowing them to 
be imported into the cell, where they will bind to and inhibit their cognate Rap phosphatase, relieving 
the inhibition of Spo0A~P. ...................................................................................................................... 6 
Fig. 5: Scheme of the evolution experiment. Sterile seedlings were pulled through a mesh and 
transferred into 100ml blue cap bottles containing 27ml of MSNg medium. They were inoculated with 
3ml of bacterial culture of OD = 0.2 and incubated in a plant chamber with a day/night cycle of 16h 
light at 24°C and 8h darkness at 21°C. After 48 h the mesh was removed from the seedling, which was 
washed in MSNg, transferred to a new bottle containing fresh media and a sterile seedling and 
incubated again for 48 hours allowing subsequent re-colonization. This transfer was repeated 19 
times. Roots 1,5,10,17 and 20 were vortexed with small glass beads to disrupt the biofilm and plated 
on LB-agar to allow CFU-counting to follow the development of the population. (B) In total four 
evolution experiments were conducted, either as mono evolution experiments, with only A. thaliana 
or Tomato seedlings, or cycling evolution experiments, where the plant species was switched after 
every third transfer. (C) Picture from the laboratory showing plants after a transfer. ........................ 15 
Fig. 6: Competition for root re-colonization between ∆hag and WT.  6-8 days old A. thaliana seedlings 
were inoculated with 1:1 mixed ∆hag mutant and WT B. subtilis, incubated for 48h in microtiter plates 
either shaking at 200 rpm or not shaking. Colonized roots were consecutively transferred twice to a 
==PAGE:7==
vi 
 
new sterile root and incubated under the same conditions. The third root was vortexed to disperse 
the biofilm and the suspension was plated on LB-agar to allow CFU counting. Bars represent the ratio 
of ∆hag cells (light blue) and WT cells (orange) on the final root. Error bars represent the standard 
error for ten replicates (N=10) under shaken and nine replicates (N=9) under non-shaken conditions. 
The mean of the ratios of ∆hag:WT cells was tested for a significant difference to a mean value of 1 
with One-sample t-Test (*** : p<0.001) A significant outlier was identified with Grubbs test in the Non-
shaken set-up and removed. ................................................................................................................ 24 
Fig. 7: Fluorescence microscopy of plant root re-colonization of B. subtilis ∆hag mutant vs. WT. A 6-
8 days old A. thaliana seedling was inoculated with 1:1 mixed mKATE-labeled ∆hag mutant and GFP-
labeled WT, and incubated under shaking conditions for 48h in a small Petri dish. Afterwards, the 
colonized root (right root) was transferred to a new Petri dish containing new medium and a sterile 
root (left root). Pictures were taken using a fluorescence microscope every 30 minutes for 50 hours 
under static conditions. Brightness and contrast of pictures were adjusted using ImageJ. ................ 25 
Fig. 8: Development of CFU per gram root during the evolution experiments. Either 14-16d old A. 
thaliana or 8-10d old Tomato seedlings were inoculated with B. subtilis with a starting OD adjusted to 
0.02, incubated for 48h under static conditions in a plant chamber and consecutively transferred to a 
new sterile root. Roots were vortexed with small glass beads after transfer 1, 5, 10, 17 and 20 to 
disrupt the biofilm and the suspension was plated on LB-agar to allow CFU counting. In evolution 
experiment 1 (A) B. subtilis was evolved only on A. thaliana; in evolution experiment 2 (B) B. subtilis 
was evolved only on Tomato; in evolution experiment 3 (C) B. subtilis was initially inoculated on A. 
thaliana and the plant species was switched after every third transfer; in evolution experiment 4 (D) 
B. subtilis was initially inoculated on Tomato and the plant species was switched after every third 
transfer. The grey lines represent the five different lineages. The thick black line represents the 
average of all five lineages. ................................................................................................................... 27 
Fig. 9: Three distinct morphology types (Wrinkle, Snow and Smooth) were observed in evolved 
populations of B. subtilis after experimental evolution on plant roots. To analyze how B. subtilis 
diversified during the experimental evolution, ON cultures were prepared from frozen stocks of 
evolved populations from root 20 of all lineages and plated on LB. Three distinct colony morphologies 
were identified (Wrinkle, Snow and Smooth) in all evolution experiments. ON cultures were prepared 
from a few randomly chosen isolates of the three morphotypes. They were plated in high density on 
LB-agar and additionally 2µl of the ON culture was spotted on LB-Agar in 9 replicates. Agar plates were 
incubated for 48h at 30°C and images were acquired using a stereomicroscope. .............................. 28 
Fig. 10: Diversification of B. subtilis populations into three distinct morphotypes (Wrinkle, Snow and 
Smooth) over time during experimental evolution on plants. To analyze how B. subtilis diversified 
during experimental evolution in plant root colonization over time, ON cultures were prepared from 
frozen stocks of evolved populations from root 5, 10, 17 and 20 of all lineages as well as of the 
ancestral strain used for the experimental evolution (“transfer 0”)  and plated on LB-agar. Plates were 
incubated for 14-16h at 30°C, and WR, SM and SN variants were identified and counted to calculate 
the ratio of a given morphotype at each time point for each lineage. Shaded areas describe the mean 
ratio of all five lineages for (A) Assay 1 (evolved only on A. thaliana); (B) Assay 2 (evolved only on 
Tomato); (C) Assay 3 (Evolved alternating on A. thaliana and Tomato) and (D) Assay 4 (evolved 
alternating on Tomato and A. thaliana) for a given morphotype of the whole population in grey for 
WR, light blue for SM and orange for SN. Error bars represent the standard error of the five lineages.
 .............................................................................................................................................................. 30 
Fig. 11 Pellicle capacity in response to plant polysaccharides of the evolved morphotypes SN, SM 
and WR in comparison to the ancestor. Pellicle capacity of the evolved morphotypes SN, SM and WR 
as well as of the ancestor was tested by inoculating LB + 0.5% xylan with ON cultures, which were 
adjusted to a final OD of 0.05, in 6-well microtiter plates. Plates were incubated at 30°C for 48h under 
==PAGE:8==
vii 
 
static conditions, to allow pellicle formation. Images of the pellicles were taken (C), followed by 
harvesting of the pellicles using an inoculation loop and transferred to a 1.5ml Eppendorf tube. The 
pellicles were centrifuged to remove remaining liquid and to measure the fresh weight (A). 
Afterwards, pellicles were dried for 72h at 60°C and the dry weight was measured (B). The relative 
biomass was calculated by dividing the weight of each replicate pellicle of the tested isolates with the 
average weight of the ancestor pellicles. Data points represent three replicates of two isolates of the 
same morphotypes or six replicates of the ancestor. Dunnett´s test was performed to test for 
significance between each morphotype and the ancestor (***: p<0.001) .......................................... 31 
Fig. 12: Colony morphology of isolates evolved only on A. thaliana compared to the ancestor on LB-
agar. To analyze the colony morphology of the evolved isolates, 2µl of an ON cultures of the evolved 
isolates (1A_1, 1A_2, 1A_3, 1C_1, 1C_2, 1C_3, 1D_1, 1D_2, 1D_3, 1E_1, 1E_2 and 1E_3) as well as of 
the ancestor were spotted on LB-agar and incubated for 48h at 30°C. Images were taken with a 
stereomicroscope set at 5X magnification. .......................................................................................... 32 
Fig. 13: Colony morphology of isolates evolved only on Tomato compared to the ancestor on LB-
agar. To analyze the colony morphology of the evolved isolates, 2µl of an ON cultures of the evolved 
isolates (2A_1, 2A_2, 2A_3, 2B_1, 2B_2, 2B_3, 2C_1, 2C_2 and 2C_3) as well as of the ancestor were 
spotted on LB-agar and incubated for 48h at 30°C. Pictures were taken with a stereomicroscope set at 
5X magnification. .................................................................................................................................. 33 
Fig. 14: Root colonization of evolved isolates, which evolved only on A. thaliana, compared to the 
ancestor. Both 14-16 days old A. thaliana (blue) and 10-12 days old Tomato seedlings (orange) were 
inoculated with different evolved isolates (1A_1, 1A_2, 1A_3, 1C_1, 1C_2, 1C_3, 1D_1, 1D_2, 1D_3, 
1E_1, 1E_2 and 1E_3) of assay 1 (evolved only on A. thaliana)  as well as the ancestor and incubated 
for 48h in a plant chamber. The biofilm on the roots was disrupted through vortexing with small glass 
beads and the suspension was plated on LB-agar to allow CFU counting. Relative root colonization was 
calculated, by dividing the log10-transformed CFU of each isolate with the mean of the log10-
transformed CFU of the ancestor. The dashed line represents the mean of the ancestor. Dunnett´s test 
was performed to test for significance between each isolate and the ancestor (* : p<0.05, **: p<0.01, 
***: p<0.001) ........................................................................................................................................ 34 
Fig. 15: Root colonization of evolved isolates, which evolved only on Tomato, compared to the 
ancestor. Both 14-16 days old A. thaliana (blue) and 8-10d old Tomato seedlings (orange) were 
inoculated with different evolved isolates (2A_1, 2A_2, 2A_3, 2B_1, 2B_2, 2B_3, 2C_1, 2C_2 and 2C_3) 
of assay 2 (evolved only on Tomato) as well as the ancestor and incubated for 48h in a plant chamber. 
The biofilm on the roots was disrupted through vortexing with small glass beads and the suspension 
was plated on LB-agar to allow CFU counting. Because different lineages were tested on multiple days, 
the ancestor was included in every experimental set-up for comparison. Relative root colonization was 
calculated by dividing the log10-transformed CFU per gram root of each replicate of the isolates with 
the mean of the log10-transformed CFU per gram root of the ancestor of the same experimental set-
up. The mean of the ancestor was set to 1 and is represented by a dashed line. Dunnett´s test was 
performed to test for significance between each isolate and the ancestor (**: p<0.01) .................... 35

Fig. 18: Relative pellicle productivity of evolved isolates compared to the ancestor. Evolved isolates 
(1A_1, 1A_2, 1A_3, 1C_1, 1C_2, 1C_3, 1D_1, 1D_2, 1D_3, 1E_1, 1E_2,1E_3, 2A_1, 2A_2, 2A_3, 2B_1,

ancestor by inoculating LB + 0.5% xylan with ON cultures, which were adjusted to a final OD of 0.05, 
in 24-well microtiter plates. Plates were incubated for 48h at 30°C under static conditions to allow 
pellicle formation. Afterwards, the pellicle was harvested with an inoculation loop, transfer to an 
Eppendorf tube and mildly sonicated to disrupt the biofilm. The bacterial suspensions were plated on 
LB-agar to allow CFU counting. Relative biofilm productivity was calculated by dividing the log10-
transformed CFU of each replicate of the isolates by the mean of the log10-transformed CFU of the 
ancestor. Dunnett´s test was performed to test for significance between each isolate and the ancestor 
(* : p<0.05, **: p<0.01). ........................................................................................................................ 39

Fig. 27: Competition in root colonization between an isolate evolved on A. thaliana (1E_3) and an

and Tomato. Both 14-16d old A. thaliana (blank) and 8-10d old Tomato seedlings (shaded) were

on Tomato, as well as a 1:1 mix of each isolate and the ancestor and incubated for 48h in a plant 
chamber. The 1:1 mix was plated on LB-agar to determine the ratio of the two strains in the initial 
inoculum. The biofilm on the roots was disrupted by vortexing with small glass beads and the 
suspension was plated on LB-agar to allow CFU counting. The number of cells colonizing the roots was 
normalized for each isolate by dividing with its starting CFU in the 1:1 mix. The ratio of colonization 
capacity was calculated by dividing the normalized CFU of each isolate of the 1:1 mix with the sum of

1. Introduction 
1.1. 
Biological control 
Pathogens and insects affecting crops are one of the greatest threats to modern food production and 
lead to huge economic losses worldwide (Pérez-Garciá et al. 2011). Because the demand for food is 
steadily increasing with the rapidly growing world population, the consequences of plant pathogens 
might even exacerbate in the coming years (Godfray et al. 2010; Tito et al. 2018). To cope with the 
threat of crop losses, often pesticides, herbicides and other agrochemicals are used excessively. 
However, such substances cause serious environmental and human health issues (Carvalho, 2017; 
Mahmood et al. 2016; Pérez-Garciá et al. 2011). In addition, the rapid emergence of resistant plant 
pathogens succeeds at this stage the pace of pesticide discovery, thereby increasing the urge of 
developing alternative strategies even further (Fisher et al. 2018). A more sustainable and innocuous 
approach to replace chemical-based control might be the usage of biocontrol agents (Barea et al. 
1996; van Lenteren et al. 2018). Indeed, biological control, in which one organism is applied to inhibit 
growth and reproduction of another organism, is believed to be one of the most environmentally safe 
as well as economically profitable plant disease and pest management methods (Cock et al. 2010, 
Heydari & Pessarakli, 2010). One highly promising candidate for agricultural applications are plant 
growth-promoting rhizobacteria (PGPR) (Bhattacharyya & Jha, 2012.).  
1.2. 
Bacillus subtilis – a plant growth-promoting rhizobacterium 
PGPR flourish in the rhizosphere, colonize plant roots and are able to enhance the plants’ performance 
in multiple ways (Barea et al. 2005). A widely studied PGPR is Bacillus subtilis, a soil-dwelling, spore-
forming Gram-positive bacterium (Cazorla et al. 2007). B. subtilis is used as a model organism to study, 
amongst other things, secondary metabolite production, surface motility, sporulation, biofilm 
development and attachment to plant roots or fungal hyphae (Kovács, 2019). In addition, it is widely 
regarded as a promising candidate for agricultural application, because of its various beneficial 
activities for important crops (Fig. 1) (Etesami & Alikhani, 2018; Lucy et al. 2004). First of all, B. subtilis 
is able to promote plant growth by increasing nutrient availability for the plant as well as improving 
soil structure and reducing abiotic stresses (Saravanakumar et al. 2011; Sivasakthi et al. 2014). 
Additionally, B. subtilis has been shown to alter the plant´s hormone homeostasis and thereby 
promoting cell division and differentiation by either producing growth hormones themselves, e.g. 
cytokinin, or by inducing their synthesis in the plant (Arkhipova et al. 2005). In addition to promoting 
plant growth, B. subtilis is able to protect the plant against pathogens in multiple ways. Especially the 
secretion of secondary metabolites might play an important role in restraining pathogens, because 
they can act as antimicrobials,  like in the case of surfactin and bacillomycin, or because they are able 
to reduce the virulence of pathogens by interfering with their quorum sensing system, as shown for 
==PAGE:15==
2 
 
lactonases (Bais et al. 2004, Dong et al. 2000). Furthermore, the sheer competition with pathogens for 
nutrients and space has been suggested to inhibit plant pathogens (Shafi et al. 2017).  Lastly, B. subtilis 
is able to trigger induced systemic resistance of the plant, whereupon local induction the defense 
capacity of the entire plant is enhanced (Lugtenberg & Kamilova, 2009; Pieterse et al. 2014).  
1.3. 
Plant root colonization by B. subtilis 
B. subtilis is commonly colonizing certain types of plant roots through biofilm formation (Cazorla et al. 
2007; Pandey & Palni; 1997). However, in agricultural applications, it seems to struggle to persist in 
the rhizosphere, if not introduced extensively (Bais et al. 2004; Shoda, 2000). Indeed, this seems to be 
one of the greatest obstacles biocontrol agents are facing. Several studies report that their efficiency 
tends to be much lower under field conditions, compared to greenhouse and laboratory trials, because 
applied strains often fail to establish themselves on the plants (Bardin et al. 2015; Maplestone & 
Campbell, 1989; Moreira & de Mio, 2015; Xu et al. 2011). It is evident that successful root colonization 
is required for the beneficial effects of bacteria to deliver their full potential in agricultural applications 
(de Weert et al. 2002). For a successful plant root colonization, certain microbial properties, e.g. 
motility and biofilm formation, seem to be essential (Allard-Massicotte et al. 2016; Benizri et al. 2001; 
Compant et al. 2010; Raina et al. 2019). A better understanding of those key properties might, 
therefore, contribute to improving the performance of applied beneficial rhizobacteria in the field. 
Fig. 1: Scheme showing the beneficial effects of PGPR on their host plant. PGBR can benefit their host 
plant either through promoting crop yield, e.g. through producing phytohormones, increasing nutrient 
availability or reducing water stress, as well as through protecting the plants against pathogens, e.g. 
through direct competition or the production and secretion of secondary metabolites with antimicrobial 
properties.  
==PAGE:16==
3 
 
1.3.1. Motility 
During the initial phase of root colonization, active and directed movement in the form of chemotaxis 
seems to be highly advantageous for PGPR to be able to establish themselves on the roots (Allard-
Massicotte et al. 2016; de Weert & Bloemberg, 2007). Chemotaxis allows bacterial cells to sense 
changes in chemical gradients around them and move accordingly to a more favorable environment, 
e.g. towards a higher nutrient concentration or away from toxins (Garrity & Ordal, 1995, Sourjik et al. 
2012; Yang et al. 2015). A chemotactic response is triggered when stimulating molecules, e.g. plant 
root exudates, bind to the corresponding chemoreceptor located on the surface of bacteria, causing 
downstream modification of the CheA kinase and of its response regulator CheY (Fig.2) (Garrity & 
Ordal, 1997). CheY in turn interacts with the flagellar motor by controlling the direction of the motor 
rotation (Bischoff et al. 1993). When no attractant is bound the flagellar motor rotates clockwise, 
which causes cells to re-orientate through tumbling, while if an attractant is bound, it rotates counter-
clockwise generating straight swimming or a so-called run (Garrity & Ordal, 1997; Larsen et al. 1974;  
Rao et al. 2004). On the other hand, how B. subtilis responds to the encounter with a repellent, is less 
understood, but it has been suggested to act directly on the membrane rather than through the CheA-
CheY pathway, leading to an increase in the tumbling frequency (Ordal & Goldman, 1976).  
Indeed, Allard-Massicotte et al. (2016) have demonstrated that multiple chemoreceptors of B. subtilis 
are involved in a response to root exudates and various mutants, which are either deficient in 
chemotaxis (∆cheA) or a-motile in general (∆hag and ∆motA), were not able to colonize A. thaliana 
roots after 4h, in contrast to the wild type (WT).  
In addition to swimming motility, B. subtilis is able to swarm, during which a whole cluster of cells 
moves rapidly on a solid surface in the form of dynamic multicellular rafts (Kearns & Losick, 2003). 
Contrary to swimming motility, it is undirected but requires the production of surfactin, which reduces 
A) 
B
Fig. 2: Simplified scheme of the chemotactic response in B. subtilis towards plant root exudates. A) The binding of an 
attractant by the chemotactic receptor CheV/CheW leads to the activation of the CheA kinase, which in turn phosphorylates 
CheY. CheY-P interacts with the motor by changing the direction of its rotation to counter-clockwise, leading to a run. B) 
Chemotaxis can be described as a biased random walk. This means bacteria are able to detect a gradient of an attractant, in 
this case, a root exudate (yellow star), and in response change the frequencies of runs, i.e. more runs towards the gradient 
and fewer runs away from the gradient. 
==PAGE:17==
4 
 
surface tension by forming a small film of water in which cells than swarm (Kearns & Losick, 2003). As 
swarming is one of the main modes of motility on solid surfaces, it might be of even higher importance 
than chemotactic swimming in a natural soil system (Gao et al. 2016). Indeed, Gao et al. (2016) 
demonstrated, that multiple B. subtilis mutants deficient in swarming (∆sfrAC, ∆swrA and ∆minJ) 
colonized only 5-15% as efficiently as the WT, emphasizing its importance for root colonization. 
1.3.2. Biofilm formation 
Once bacterial cells reached the root, the ability to form stable biofilms, allowing tight attachment, 
plays an important role for successful root colonization (Dragoš et al. 2018b; Meneses et al. 2011) (Fig. 
1). Biofilms are one of the most successful forms of life and are found in a wide range of environments 
(Flemming et al. 2016). They consist of cells, which are packed tightly together, embedded in a self-
produced extracellular matrix (ECM), which consists of exopolysaccharides, structural proteins and 
extracellular DNA (Nadell et al. 2008). In B. subtilis the ECM is composed mainly of an 
exopolysaccharide (EPS) and the protein TasA (Branda et al. 2006). Indeed, Dragoš et al. (2018b) have 
shown that both EPS and TasA are required for successful root colonization, as both null mutants, Δeps 
and ΔtasA, showed a reduced number of cells on plants, compared to the WT. The production of EPS 
and proteins is often triggered by external cues, including specific nutrients as well as signals from 
hosts, competitors or co-operators (Beauregard et al. 2013; Dragoš et al. 2018b; Oliveira et al. 2015).  
Beauregard et al. (2013) observed that the plant polysaccharides arabinogalactan, pectin and xylan 
induced pellicle formation of B. subtilis.   
In addition to its necessity in root colonization, a dense biofilm formed on the roots is crucial to the 
beneficial effects PGPR have on plants. First of all, because biofilms are highly resistant against 
invasions of other microbes, they are able to functions as a barrier against pathogens (Bogino et al. 
Fig. 3: Schematic presentation showing the cycle of biofilm formation of B. subtilis on plant roots. During the initiation of
biofilm formation on a plant root, motile cells with flagella attach and differentiate into non-motile matrix-producing cells,
which start to form a monolayer of cells sticking together surrounded by extracellular matrix. Cells grow further and produce
more matrix to form microcolonies, which eventually develop into a mature biofilm. During maturation cells inside the 
biofilm differentiate into distinct cell types including sporulating cells, competent cells and motile cells. Upon environmental,
e.g. change in nutrient levels, or internal cues, e.g. quorum sensing, cells start to actively leave the matrix and the biofilm 
disperses.  
==PAGE:18==
5 
 
2013; Molina-Santiago et al. 2019; Mousa et al. 2016). Secondly, it has been shown that EPS 
production by rhizosphere bacteria improves soil structure and aids plants to avoid water stress 
(Awasthi et al. 2017). 
Even though biofilms are often thought to be robust structures consisting mainly of matrix producing 
cells, mature B. subtilis biofilms are quite heterogeneous and many different phenotypes apart from 
matrix producers can be present even in mono-species communities, including sporulating, 
competent, cannibal, mining as well as motile cells (Abee et al. 2011, Mielich‐Süss & Lopez, 2015). 
Through this specification in performing different tasks, division of labor is allowed, which assures that 
resources are used efficiently and the whole community benefits (Van Gestel et al. 2015). 
Furthermore, it allows individual cells to actively leave the matrix and colonize new surfaces, which 
most likely enables bacteria to evade stress caused by overpopulation (Parsek & Greenberg, 2005; 
McDougald et al. 2012). 
1.3.3. Cell differentiation and quorum sensing 
In B. subtilis three master regulators have been identified as key players controlling cell differentiation, 
namely DegU for exoprotease secreting, ComA for competent and surfactin producing and Spo0A for 
matrix producing and sporulating cells (Dahl et al. 1992; Hamon et al. 2001; Kunst et al. 1994; Mielich‐
Süss & Lopez, 2015). All three master regulators are activated upon phosphorylation by their 
corresponding kinases, which respond to specific external cues (Bendori et al. 2015; Burbulys et al. 
1991). This ensures that the correct cellular response is only carried out under the right environmental 
conditions. Spo0A, for example, is activated through a phosphorylation cascade initiated by at least 
four different kinases (KinA-D), which all respond to different external signals, like compounds 
released by plant roots in the case of KinCD, or low oxygen and high iron in the case of KinAB 
(Beauregard et al. 2013; Jiang et al. 2000b; Kolodkin-Gal et al. 2013). In addition, a fifth kinase namely 
KinE is also part of this pathway, however, its exact function is unclear (McLoon et al. 2011b). Spo0A 
controls the reversible switch from motile single cells to biofilm formation through a double-negative 
feedback loop involving SlrR, SinR and SinI (Kearns et al. 2005; Vlamakis et al. 2013) (Fig. 4). This switch, 
and therefore also Spo0A, is of particular interest in plant root colonization because it marks an 
essential step during the establishment of bacteria on the plant (Chen et al. 2013). The repression of 
either matrix or motility genes depends thereby on a low-SlrR or high-SlrR state, respectively (Chu et 
al. 2008; Kobayashi, 2008). During biofilm formation, the switch from low to high state is induced by 
the production of SinI, which depends on the phosphorylation level of Spo0A~P (Chai et al. 2008). If 
Spo0A is unphosphorylated, sinI is not expressed, and SinR represses slrR keeping the levels of SlrR 
low, which enables SinR to repress matrix genes, e.g. eps and tasA (Kearns et al. 2005). During 
relatively low levels of Spo0A~P, the high-affinity activator of sinI is bound, activating the production 
==PAGE:19==
6 
 
of SinI, which leads to an inhibition of SinR and therefore high levels of SlrR (Chai et al. 2008). In the 
high-SlrR state, SlrR binds to SinR and derepresses matrix genes, while at the same time the formed 
SlrR-SinR complex represses the motility gene hag and autolysin genes, resulting thereby in sessile, 
coherent matrix producing cells (Bai et al. 1993; Chai et al. 2010; Chu et al. 2008). As the 
phosphorylation level of Spo0A~P increases, other low-affinity operators of sinI are bound and further 
production of SinI is reduced, while at the same time sporulation genes are activated (Fujita et al. 
2005). In addition, an alternative matrix gene repressor AbrB, which also targets eps and tasA, is 
connected to the Spo0A pathway, adding thereby more complexity and enabling further fine-tuning 
of the expression of matrix-related genes (Chu et al. 2008; Vlamakis et al. 2013). AbrB will be expressed 
at low Spo0A-level and inhibits matrix genes additionally to SinR (Chu et al. 2008). At intermediate 
level, Spo0A~P represses the abrB gene while inducing expression of AbbA, which inhibits the AbrB 
present in the cell and thereby relieving the repression of matrix genes (Chu et al. 2008). The same 
core regulatory genes of this pathway have been shown to be essential for successful root 
colonization, as various null mutations inside the Spo0A pathways led to either defective biofilms with 
a decreased number of cells attached to the root (ΔsinI, Δeps and ΔtasA) or hyper-robust biofilms with 
a higher number of cells (ΔabrB and ΔsinR), depending on whether they positively or negatively 
regulate biofilm formation, respectively (Chen et al. 2013).  
Fig. 4: Simplified scheme of the switch between motility and matrix production in B. subtilis under the control of 
Spo0A. Plant signals are recognized by the sensor kinases KinC and KinD, initiating a phosphorylation cascade to 
activate Spo0A~P. Upon phosphorylation, Spo0A~P induces the production of SinI and thereby represses SinR and 
derepression of slrR. This results in high levels of SlrR, which bind to SinR, derepressing matrix genes, while at the 
same time the formed SlrR-SinR complex represses motility genes. In addition, Spo0A~P represses AbrB and therefore 
relieves matrix genes. The Spo0A pathway is further regulated through the quorum sensing system, including the 
autoinducer Phr and the rap phosphatases, which negatively regulate the phosphorylation cascade of Spo0A. The 
autoinducer Phr is translated as Pre-Phr proteins, which are secreted and processed into mature Phr peptides. At
high cell density, Phr will reach a threshold concentration allowing them to be imported into the cell, where they will 
bind to and inhibit their cognate Rap phosphatase, relieving the inhibition of Spo0A~P. 
==PAGE:20==
7 
 
 
To even further-fine tune cell differentiation, all three master regulators are additionally controlled 
through quorum sensing (Bendori et al. 2015). Quorum sensing is a mean to enable cell-cell 
communication on the base of the production, secretion and response to an autoinducer (Duanis-
Assaf et al. 2016). This enables cells to detect the cellular density of other producers and thereby 
potential co-operators around them and respond accordingly (Waters & Bassler 2005). This is a key 
requirement for the development of cooperative behavior, because it stabilizes the production of a 
public good, like surfactin or extracellular matrix components (Abisado et al. 2018; Stevens et al. 2012; 
von Bodman et al. 2008). The autoinducer of B. subtilis are Phr peptides, which bind to and inhibit the 
Rap phosphatases that control the three master regulators by altering their state of phosphorylation 
(Bendori et al. 2015). In the case of Spo0A, the phosphorylation cascade is negatively regulated by Rap 
phosphatases RapABEHIJ60, which in turn are inhibited by quorum sensing peptides Phr (Jiang et al. 
2000a; Perego & Hoch, 1996).  
Because all three master regulators are linked to the Rap proteins, quorum sensing in B. subtilis 
controls swarming motility and biofilm formation as well as sporulation and competence (Kalamara et 
al. 2018). Furthermore, experimental evidence implies that also biofilm dispersal is controlled through 
quorum sensing, highlighting its important role during re-colonization (Parsek & Greenberg, 2005).  
1.4. 
Plant-microbial interactions 
In addition to intraspecies signaling systems, biofilm formation and motility are driven by interactions 
with the host plant (Sasse et al. 2018). It is evident that root exudates act as a signal for chemotactic 
responses and therefore influence bacterial motility greatly (Allard-Massicotte et al. 2016; Feng et al. 
2018; Scharf et al. 2016). In addition, certain plant polysaccharides located on the surface of the plant 
cell wall have been shown to trigger biofilm formation in B. subtilis (Beauregard et al. 2013). 
Experimental evidence shows that plants are indeed able to control the composition of their 
microbiome at least to some extent and recruit protective microorganisms actively (Pieterse et al. 
2014; Yi et al. 2011). Many factors are involved in shaping the plant microbiome and studies imply 
that the early plant microbiome itself also plays an important role (Toju et al. 2018; Wei et al. 2019). 
One way the plant itself can regulate this is through the production and secretion of various root 
exudates, which either act as attractants or repellents, as well as growth stimulants or inhibitors and 
therefore enable the plant to select desired microbes (Berendsen et al. 2012). The chemical properties 
of root exudates can thereby vary greatly between distinct plant species (Shann & Boyle, 1994). 
Indeed, Zhang et al. (2014) observed indications that PGPR prefer colonization of their original host 
plant species based on root exudate components. In accordance with this, it has been shown that the 
host plant species is a major cause of the variation in the composition of the root microbiome 
==PAGE:21==
8 
 
(Fitzpatrick et al. 2017). Together, this might indicate that beneficial rhizobacteria have to adapt 
differently to be able to thrive in the rhizospheres of distinct plant species (Micallef et al. 2009). 
However, it is not known, if and how a single bacterial species adapts specifically to successfully 
colonize plant roots of distinct plant species. One way to study this is through experimental evolution.  
1.5. 
Experimental evolution  
In general, experimental evolution is a powerful tool to analyze how organisms adapt to a wide range 
of environments, to study evolutionary trade-offs and constraints and ultimately to test evolutionary 
theories (Kawecki et al. 2012). This is especially applicable for microorganisms because their short 
generation times and the possibility to store them as frozen stocks for later comparisons with the 
ancestral strain makes them ideal candidates for long term experiments with many generations (Elena 
& Lenski, 2003). The general approach of experimental evolution is to allow cells to grow until the 
population reaches a high density but instead of throwing out or using all of the remaining cells, part 
of the culture is transferred to new medium or directly diluted (McDonald, 2019). This allows 
continued growth and therefore natural selection will drive the population over generations to adapt 
to the given conditions (McDonald, 2019). How the population develops and diversifies over the 
course of evolution depends thereby on the availability of distinct niches (Travisano & Rainey, 2000). 
In an unstructured homogenous environment, e.g. well-mixed planktonic cultures, beneficial 
mutations are expected to take over the population leading to a sequential replacement over time 
(Habets et al. 2006). However, evolutionary models studying biofilms present a slightly different story. 
Here, biofilms allow the existence of heterogeneous microenvironments, as various gradients might 
occur, for example in oxygen and nutrients (De Beer et al. 1994, Stewart & Franklin, 2008). Those 
gradients combined with the spatial structure prevalent in biofilm communities provide vacant niches, 
which are accessible to novel genotypes (Yoder et al. 2010). Therefore, populations diversify often in 
evolutionary biofilm studies and distinct morphological variants, so-called morphotypes, are allowed 
to co-exist (Korona et al. 1994; Poltak & Cooper, 2011; McDonald et al. 2009; Rainey & Travisano, 
1998). Furthermore, the plant root itself might construct heterogeneous microenvironments through 
different concentrations of root exudates at distinct plant compartments, e.g. root collar, root hair 
zone or root tip, providing even more vacant niches (Bais et al. 2006; Rovira 1969).  
By using experimental evolution of a bacterium on different plant species, one can identify how it 
adapts in root colonization, and whether these adaptations are plant species-specific.  
 
 
==PAGE:22==
9 
 
2. Research aims 
This study consisted of three parts.  
First of all, a review summarizing the current knowledge about plant-microbial interactions of the B. 
subtilis species, including the diverse mechanisms for supporting plant growth as well as the 
necessities for the establishment of the relationship, has been drafted and prepared for submission 
to a scientific journal (see Supplementary, 9.1.).  
Secondly, a pre-experiment was conducted, using an a-motile Δhag mutant, to investigate, whether 
motility is essential for successful root colonization. Therefore, the Δhag mutant was studied in direct 
competition with B. subtilis WT for root colonization on A. thaliana seedlings in liquid media under 
both, shaken and static conditions.   
Lastly, the main part of this study consisted of an evolution experiment with B. subtilis, with the aim 
to select for root colonization by continuously transferring a previously colonized seedling to fresh 
medium containing a new sterile seedling. Therefore, only bacterial cells, which were successful in 
colonization were transferred to the next cycle. Performing this in either mono or cycling assays with 
two distinct plant species, namely Arabidopsis thaliana and Tomato, not only allows to investigate, 
which bacterial traits are important for improvements in root colonization but also if bacteria adapt 
differently to distinct plant species. This includes, whether B. subtilis behaves as a generalist, i.e. 
where evolution on one plant species will also confer improved properties on other plant species, or 
as a specialist, where adaptation to one plant species only confers improved properties on that plant 
species. 
 
The overall aim of this project is to investigate, how B. subtilis evolves in root colonization on two 
distinct plant species and what role key bacterial properties like motility and biofilm formation play in 
root (re-) colonization. In this context, the following questions were proposed for this study: 
1. Is motility essential for successful root colonization of B. subtilis and will an a-motile Δhag 
mutant be outcompeted by the motile Wild type strain? 
2. Is B. subtilis able to adapt rapidly in root colonization and improve in biofilm formation and/or 
motility?  
3. Will B. subtilis adapt specifically towards the plant species it evolved on, or do general 
adaptations evolve, which confer an advantage on both plant species? 
4. Do populations diversify when evolving on plant roots, and if so how do distinct variants 
interact with each other? 
 
 
 
==PAGE:23==
10 
 
3. Model plants 
3.1. 
Arabidopsis thaliana 
A. thaliana is one of the, if not the, most studied plant model organism, because of its fast growth, 
easy handling and low space requirements (Leonelli, 2007). It is a member of the Brassicaceae family 
and has a broad natural distribution throughout Europe, Asia, and North America (Meyerowitz & 
Sommerville, 1994). The entire life cycle of A. thaliana, starting from seed germination to the 
development of the first leaves and the main stem until flowering and the maturation of seeds is 
completed in around 6 weeks (Meinke et al., 1998). Fully developed plants have a diameter of around 
2 to 10 cm, a height of up to 30cm – 40cm with flowers of 2 mm and seeds of 0.5 mm (Meyerowitz, 
1987). Plants can be grown in Petri plates or maintained in pots located either in a greenhouse or 
under fluorescent lights in the laboratory (Langridge, 1957). The root system consists of a hierarchical 
tree structure with one primary main root producing lateral roots, which in turn produce higher-order 
lateral roots (Petricka et al., 2012). A. thaliana has been used as a model organism to study various 
research areas, including interactions with microorganisms, both PGPR and pathogens (Stringlis et al. 
2019). However, its most important field of research might still lie in genome analysis, with the 
ambitious aim to update and complete the genomic map of A. thaliana and thereby establish a 
comprehensive dataset of all known genes with a loss‐of‐function phenotype (Koornneef & Meinke, 
2010).  
3.2. 
Tomato 
Tomato has been more and more recognized as a good alternate model plant, especially to analyze 
plant-pathogen interactions (Arie et al. 2007). It originated in the Andean regions of Peru, Chile, and 
Ecuador and is by now one of the most popular vegetables worldwide (Blanca et al. 2012). Tomato 
belongs to the Solanaceae family, which is extremely large and comprises of a lot of important plant 
species used for food and medical applications (Kimura & Sinha, 2008). It takes approximately 12 to 
16 weeks from germination until the Tomato plant is fully developed and bears ripe fruits (Kimura & 
Sinha, 2008). In contrast to A. thaliana, Tomato plants have a sympodial shoot development, meaning 
the primary vegetative shoot terminates in a flower and a new vegetative shoot arises from the axillary 
bud just below (Kimura & Sinha, 2008). Therefore, the plant size can vary in height between 1 to 3 
meters. Tomato plants have a similar root architecture as A. thaliana with a primary root which 
subsequently produces lateral roots (Muday & Haworth, 1994). Apart from plant-microbe interaction, 
Tomato plants are an especially important model plant to study metabolomics and proteomics of ruit 
bearing crops (de Vos et al. 2018; Sant’Ana et al. 2018).  
==PAGE:24==
11 
 
4. Methods 
4.1. 
Bacterial strains, strain construction and media 
For this study, B. subtilis strain NCBI 3610 DK1042 and derived mutants listed in Table 1 were used 
(Tab.1). The strain 3610 is an undomesticated strain that still exhibits important phenotypes like 
pellicle formation, swarming motility and the production of secondary metabolites, which are 
otherwise lost in domesticated strains (McLoon et al. 2011a). The 3610 DK1042 strain additionally 
harbors a mutation in the comI gene making it natural competent (Nye et al. 2017). Apart from the 
Wild-type B. subtilis strain NCBI 3610 DK1042 (WT or ancestor in the following), red and green 
fluorescent-labeled (mKATE and GFP, respectively) derivatives were used. 
In addition, as part of this study, a green and red fluorescent-labeled Δhag mutant were used. The hag 
gene encodes the flagellin structural protein in B. subtilis (Mirel & Chamberlin, 1989). The deficiency 
of the mutant to produce flagellin, which is the basic component of the Flagella, leads therefore to 
unflagellated and non-motile cells (Mirel & Chamberlin, 1989).  
All strains were pre-grown overnight (ON culture), by picking up cells with a sterile pipette tip from 
frozen stock and inoculating 2ml of Lennox-Broth (LB) (10 g/L Tryptone, 5 g/L Yeast Extract and 5 g/L 
NaCl) in sterile culture tubes. When necessary, spectinomycin was used at 100µg/ml. The tubes were 
incubated at 37°C at 220 rpm shaking for 14-16h.  
In addition, specifically chosen evolved isolates (1E_3 and 2A_1) were transformed via natural 
competence transformation using plasmids carrying genes for either green or red fluorescent protein, 
as well as a resistance gene against spectinomycin as selective marker (pWK-Sp hy-GFP and pWK-Sp 
hy-mKATE, respectively). Therefore, genetic material has been obtained from an E.coli MC1061 strain 
carrying the plasmid using a standard plasmid isolation Kit (High Pure Plasmid Isolation Kit, Roche). To 
induce competence in bacterial cells, 10µl of ON cultures of the evolved isolates was transferred to 
2ml of competence medium (10% 10×MC (10.7 g K2HPO4, 5.2 g KH2PO4, 20 g dextrose, 0.88 g sodium 
citrate dehydrate, 2.2 g l-glutamic acid monopotassium salt, 1 ml 1,000× ferric ammonium citrate, and 
1 g casein hydrolysate per 100 ml.), 0.05% trp and 3.35mM MgSO4) and incubated for 4-5h at 37°C 
shaking at 220rpm (Konkol et al. 2013). Afterwards, 400µl of the bacterial suspension has been 
transferred to a fresh sterile culture tube and either 10µl of the plasmid or 10µl of water control has 
been added. Tubes were incubated for an additional 2h at 37°C at 220rpm shaking. Afterwards, 50µl, 
100µl and 200µl of the suspension have been plated on LB-Agar supplemented with spectinomycin, 
to select for successful transformants. After incubating for 14-16h at 37°C, bacterial colonies were 
tested for successful transformation by picking the colony with a sterile inoculation loop and re-
streaking on LB-agar supplemented with spectinomycin, as well as on 1% starch agar plates, to test 
for amylase-negative phenotypes, because amylase activity should be lost, if the plasmid was correctly 
==PAGE:25==
12 
 
introduced. Finally, successful transformation was validated by testing for fluorescence under a 
stereomicroscope (Axio Zoom V16, ZEISS) equipped with a Zeiss CL 9000 LED light source and an 
Axiocam 503 mono (ZEISS). A frozen stock of successful transformants was prepared by mixing 900µl 
of ON culture with 100µl of 85% Glycerol in 1.5ml Cryotubes and stored at -80°C.

Other medium used during this thesis is a minimal salts nitrogen medium (MSN) (5mM potassium 
phosphate buffer pH7, 0.1M Mops pH7, 4mM MgCl2 ,0.05mM MnCl2,1μM ZnCl2,2μM thiamine, 700μM 
CaCl2, 0.2% NH4Cl) supplemented with either 0.05% glycerol (MSNg, for root colonization 
experiments) or 0.5% cellobiose (MSNc, for growth in response to plant polysaccharides). 
4.2. 
Preparation of seedlings 
A. thaliana (ecotype Col-0) was grown in soil for six weeks in a plant chamber (16h light at 24 °C and 
8h darkness at  21°C). Seeds were harvested, dried for two weeks and stored at 4°C in the absence of 
light. Tomato seeds were ordered and stored at room temperature in the absence of light (Solanum 
lycopersicum L., Maja BuschTomato, Buzzy Seeds, NL).  
Seeds were surface sterilized in Eppendorf tubes by shaking with an orbital mixer for 12 minutes in 
1ml 2% NaOCl. Afterwards, seeds were washed five times in 1ml sterile MiliQ (MQ). For A. thaliana 8-
10 seeds were plated on pre-dried 1% MS-agar (Murashige and Skoog basal salts mixture; Sigma) 
==PAGE:26==
13 
 
(2.2 g l−1) ensuring that the seedlings have sufficient space for proper root development. The plates 
were sealed with parafilm and stratified in the dark at 4°C for 2-3d. Afterwards, the plates were 
incubated in a plant chamber for either 4-6d or 12-14d at an angle of 65°. For Tomato 6-8 seeds were 
plated on 1% MS-agar and the plates were sealed with parafilm and incubated in a plant chamber for 
8-10d. The plant chamber was set to a day/night cycle of 16h light at 24°C and 8°C darkness at 21°C 
4.3. 
Experimental evolution  
The major experiment of this project was the evolution experiment of B. subtilis in root colonization 
on both A. thaliana and Tomato plants. Overall, four different evolution experiments (1-4) with five 
lineages (A-E) each were set up. These consisted of two mono evolution experiments, one only 
evolving on A. thaliana (1) and the other only on Tomato (2), as well as two cycling evolution 
experiments, which either started on A. thaliana (3) or Tomato (4) and switched the plant species after 
every third transfer (Fig 5b). At the start of the experiment, sterile seedlings were inoculated with B. 
subtilis 3610 DK1042 in sterile MSNg and incubated for 48h (Fig. 5a). Every other day, a previously 
colonized seedling was transferred to a new sterile seedling. Therefore, only bacterial cells successful 
in colonizing the roots continued in the experiment, allowing for a selection of a regular cycle of 
chemotaxis towards the root, biofilm formation on the root and dispersal. By using a minimal salt 
medium, we ensured that bacterial growth is solely dependent on root exudates produced by the 
seedlings and bacterial cells are stimulated to colonize plants to utilize resources efficiently. Because 
a pre-experiment with an a-motile mutant conducted as part of this study revealed that motility is an 
essential trait under static conditions, but the disadvantage of a-motility is lost under shaken 
conditions, the evolution experiment was conducted under static conditions.  
To set up the evolution experiment, ten homogenous seedlings of either 14-16d old A. thaliana or 8-
10d old Tomato were selected. To ensure only the roots provide a surface for colonization and not 
leaves or sprout, the seedlings were carefully pulled through a sterile square mesh with an area of 
around 9cm² and width of 0.045″×0.025″ (Stretchable High-Temperature Plastic Mesh, McMaster-
Carr). Afterwards, the seedling was carefully transferred into 100ml glass reagent bottles containing 
27ml of MSNg medium, ensuring that the mesh was floating on the surface and the roots were in the 
medium, while the sprout and leaves were above. 
An ON culture of the ancestral strain B.subtilis 3610 DK1042 was prepared on the day prior to the set-
up. The next morning, it was diluted 100-fold, to let the culture re-grow for 3-4h at 37°C until it reached 
the exponential phase. The Optical density (OD) of the culture was measured and adjusted to 0.2 in 
MSNg. Each of the flasks containing a sterile seedling was inoculated with 3ml of bacterial culture, to 
reach a starting OD of 0.02, and incubated under static conditions for 48h in a plant chamber to allow 
initial root colonization. The plant chamber was set to a day/night cycle of 16h light at 24°C and 8h 
==PAGE:27==
14 
 
darkness at 21°C. After this point, every 48h, fresh blue cap bottles were prepared by adding 30ml of 
MSNg and a new sterile seedling to each flask. For the transfer, the mesh of the previously colonized 
seedling was carefully removed, the seedling was carefully washed to remove non-colonizing cells by 
dipping it 8-10 times into a well containing sterile MSNg and subsequently transferred to a bottle 
containing fresh media and a new sterile seedling. The flasks were incubated under the same 
conditions for 48h to allow re-colonization. This transfer was conducted 19 times in total, so that in 
the end for each lineage 20 seedlings had been colonized. 
==PAGE:28==
15 
 
Fig. 5: Scheme of the evolution experiment. Sterile seedlings were pulled through a mesh and transferred into 100ml blue cap bottles containing 27ml of MSNg medium. They were 
inoculated with 3ml of bacterial culture of OD = 0.2 and incubated in a plant chamber with a day/night cycle of 16h light at 24°C and 8h darkness at 21°C. After 48 h the mesh was 
removed from the seedling, which was washed in MSNg, transferred to a new bottle containing fresh media and a sterile seedling and incubated again for 48 hours allowing 
subsequent re-colonization. This transfer was repeated 19 times. Roots 1,5,10,17 and 20 were vortexed with small glass beads to disrupt the biofilm and plated on LB-agar to allow 
CFU-counting to follow the development of the population. (B) In total four evolution experiments were conducted, either as mono evolution experiments, with only A. thaliana or 
Tomato seedlings, or cycling evolution experiments, where the plant species was switched after every third transfer. (C) Picture from the laboratory showing plants after a transfer.

                      Transfer 2 
 
                           Transfer 3                                            Transfer 20
