==PAGE:29==
16 
 
4.4. 
Population dynamics during evolution experiment 
4.4.1. CFU during experimental evolution 
To follow the development of the populations, the community of bacterial cells colonizing the roots 
was plated on LB-agar for root 1, 5, 10, 17 and 20. Therefore, the roots of the seedlings, which had 
previously undergone both colonization and re-colonization, were detached from the sprout, 
transferred to 2ml Eppendorf tubes containing 1ml of 0,9% NaCl and 200µl of small glass beads (Glass 
beads Ø0.75-1.00mm, Roth) and vortexed vigorously for 3 minutes. This allowed for efficient 
disruption of the biofilm on plant roots, without sonication, thereby minimizing negative effects on 
cell-viability. Afterwards, dilution series from 1000-fold to 10000-fold were prepared and 100µl of 
each dilution was plated out on LB-Agar in technical triplicates. LB-plates were incubated for 14-16h 
at 30°C and colony-forming units (CFU) were counted.  
The rest of the bacterial suspension of root 5, 10, 17 and 20 was used to prepare a frozen stock, as 
previously described, for later comparison experiments. Furthermore, the roots were dried with a 
paper towel and weighed, to determine the fresh weight of the root and therefore the number of cells 
colonizing per gram of root. 
4.4.2. Diversification of evolved population 
To analyze if and how evolved populations diversified, ON cultures of the frozen stock from transfer 
5, 10, 17 and 20 were prepared for each lineage and incubated 14-16h. In addition, one ON culture of 
the ancestor was prepared and incubated for 14-16h, to characterize the initial starting population. 
The cultures were diluted 106-fold in 0.9% NaCl, plated on LB-Agar and incubated at 30°C for 14-16h. 
Afterwards, different morphotypes of the populations were identified according to distinct 
characteristics in colony morphology and counted to determine their relative proportion. To ensure 
different morphotypes are genetically stable, random colonies of each variant were picked with a 
sterile pipette tip to prepare fresh ON cultures, which in turn were re-streaked twice on LB-agar.  
4.4.3. Biofilm capacity of distinct morphotypes 
To analyze the biofilm biomass of distinct morphotypes, pellicle formation was tested in response to 
the plant polysaccharide xylan. Therefore, ON of distinct morphotypes and the ancestor were 
prepared and the OD was adjusted to 5.0 in LB + 0.5% xylan. Afterwards, 4ml of LB + 0.5% xylan were 
transferred into each well of a six well-plate and the wells were inoculated by 40µl of bacterial culture. 
Well plates were incubated at 30°C for 48h and pictures were taken with a camera (Lumix DC T290, 
Panasonic equipped with lens DC Vario-ELMAR, Leica). Afterwards, the pellicle was carefully harvested 
with a sterile inoculation loop by gently penetrating the edge to partially detach the pellicle from the 
wall of the plastic well and then rapidly moving the loop along at the wall of the well. Pellicles were 
transferred to pre-weight 1.5ml Eppendorf tubes and centrifuged for 5min at 8000rpm. The 
supernatant was removed and fresh weight of pellicles was determined by subtracting the weight of 
==PAGE:30==
17 
 
the empty tube from the tube containing the pellicle. Tubes were placed in an incubator at 60°C for 
72h to ensure full evaporation of remaining liquid. Dry weight of pellicles was determined by 
subtracting the weight of the empty tube from the tube containing the dried pellicle. Two isolates of 
each distinct morphotype, the ancestor and a blank media control were tested in triplicates. Positions 
of different morphotypes were randomized on the 6-well plate. The relative biomass was calculated 
by dividing either the fresh or dry weight of pellicles of each tested strain with the mean of the fresh 
or dry weight of the ancestor pellicle. Overall two isolates of each morphotype and the ancestor have 
been tested in triplicates (Supp. 1) 
4.5. 
Evolved strains 
4.5.1. Isolation of evolved strains 
To test, whether B. subtilis improved in specific root colonization properties after the evolution 
experiment, overall 21 colonies were isolated. These consisted of three isolates each from four 
different lineages, which evolved only on A. thaliana (1A, 1C, 1D and 1E), as well as from three 
different lineages, which evolved only on Tomato (2A, 2B and 2C) (three isolates per lineage). 
Therefore, ON cultures from the frozen stock of the populations of transfer 20 were prepared and 
diluted 106-fold in 0.9% NaCl. Bacterial suspensions were plated on LB-Agar and incubated at 30°C for 
14-16h. Colonies were picked randomly with a sterile pipette tip and fresh ON cultures were prepared. 
After incubation, the cultures were clean-streaked to ensure the culture is homogeneous and a frozen 
stock was prepared and stored at -80°C.  
4.5.2. Colony morphology 
To characterize the morphology of the evolved strains, 2µl of ON culture was spotted on 1.5% LB-Agar. 
For each evolved isolate as well as for the ancestor as control, three plates were prepared, each with 
three spots, ensuring enough space for proper colony development. Plates were incubated for 48h at 
30°C. Images were taken under a stereomicroscope set at 5x magnification. Overall, 12 isolates, which 
evolved only on A. thaliana, as well as 9 isolates, which evolved only on Tomato, were tested for 
changes in colony morphology, compared to the ancestor (Supp. 1). 
4.5.3. Root colonization  
In order to analyze, if the evolved strains show improved root colonization compared to the ancestor, 
seedlings of both A. thaliana and Tomato were inoculated with either the evolved isolates or the 
ancestral strain following the same protocol as previously described for the experimental evolution 
(see 4.3.). After incubating them for 48h in the plant chamber, the roots of the seedlings were, washed 
in sterile MSNg, transferred to 2ml Eppendorf tubes containing 1ml 0.9% NaCl and vortexed with small 
glass beads. The bacterial suspension was diluted in 0.9% NaCl and plated on LB-Agar. Plates were 
incubated at 30°C for 14-16h and CFU were counted. Overall, root colonization was tested in three to 
==PAGE:31==
18 
 
four replicates for 12 different isolates evolved on A. thaliana and 9 isolates evolved on Tomato, on 
both plant species, compared to the ancestor (Supp. 1). Because different lineages were tested on 
multiple days, the ancestor was included in each experimental set-up for comparison. Relative root 
colonization was calculated by dividing the log10-transformed CFU on roots of each strain by the mean 
of the log10-transformed CFU of the ancestor from the same experimental set-up. 
4.5.4. Biofilm formation 
To further analyze the ability of the evolved strains to form a biofilm in response to plant compounds, 
pellicle formation was tested in LB + 0.5% xylan. Therefore, the OD of ON cultures was adjusted to 5.0 
in LB + 0.5% xylan. Afterwards, 1ml of LB + 0.5% xylan was transferred into each well of a 24-well plate 
and they were inoculated with 15µl of bacterial culture. Evolved isolates as well as the ancestor and a 
blank media control were tested in triplicates and their position on the well plate was randomized. 
Plates were incubated for 48h at 30°C to allow the pellicles to develop. Images were taken under a 
stereomicroscope set at 3.2× magnification. The pellicle was then carefully harvested, as previously 
described (see 4.4.3.) and transferred to an Eppendorf tube containing 1ml 0.9%NaCl. Pellicles were 
mildly sonicated using a protocol optimized in our lab consisting of 3 cycles with 12 seconds intervals 
of 1s pulses and 1s pauses (Vibra-Cell processor VCX 130, Sonics), which allows for proper disruption 
of the biofilm without affecting cell-viability (Martin et al. 2017). The bacterial solutions were diluted 
in 0.9%NaCl, plated on LB-agar and incubated at 30°C for 14-16h to allow counting of pellicle CFU. 
Overall, 12 isolates, which evolved only on A. thaliana, as well as 9 isolates, which evolved only on 
Tomato, were tested in triplicates for improvements in biofilm formation (Supp. 1). Because different 
lineages were tested on multiple days, the ancestor was included in each experimental set-up for 
comparison. Relative biofilm productivity was calculated by dividing the log10-transformed CFU of 
each isolate by the mean of the log10-transformed CFU of the ancestor of the same experimental set-
up. 
4.5.3. Motility 
In order to test, whether motility of evolved stains was improved during the evolution on plant roots, 
both swimming and swarming assays were set up. Therefore, 0.3% LB-Agar and 0.7% LB-Agar plates 
were prepared for swimming and swarming, respectively. To ensure the right level of humidity for 
swimming and swarming plates, exactly 20ml of LB-Agar was poured at a temperature of 60°C into 
Petri dishes. Afterwards, plates were dried with the lit off for 5 minutes or 20 minutes for swimming 
and swarming, respectively. The OD of ON cultures was adjusted 5.0 in LB and each plate was 
inoculated by spotting 2µl of culture in the middle of the plate. Plates were incubated at 30°C and 
images were taken for swimming after 6h, 8h and 24h and for swarming after 8h, 10h and 24h with a 
camera (Lumix DC T290, Panasonic equipped with lens DC Vario-ELMAR, Leica). The area the bacterial 
==PAGE:32==
19 
 
cells covered at a given time point as well as the area of the whole agar plate were measured using 
ImageJ (Fiji ImageJ 1.52p). The capacity in swimming and swarming motility was calculated by dividing 
the covered area by the total area. Overall, 7 isolates, which evolved only on A. thaliana, as well as 5 
isolates, which evolved only on Tomato, were tested in triplicates for changes in swimming and 
swarming motility, compared to the ancestor (Supp. 1). 
Furthermore, evolved isolates were tested in changes in a chemotactic response towards root 
exudates in capillary assays (adjusted to, Allard-Massicotte et al. 2016). To collect root exudates 10d 
old Tomato or 16d old A. thaliana seedlings were transferred to a 24-well plate containing 1.5ml of 
MSNg medium. The plate was incubated in the plant chamber for 14d shaking at 150rpm. Afterwards, 
the plants were removed and the contents of the wells were filtered with a 0.45-µM Millipore filter 
and stored at 4°C. To test the chemotactic response, ON cultures of evolved isolates were centrifuged 
for 5min at 8000 rpm and washed twice in a chemotaxis buffer [10 mM potassium phosphate buffer 
(pH 7.0), 0.1 mM EDTA, 0.05% glycerol, 5 mM sodium-D,L-lactate, 0.14 mM CaCl2, and 0.3 mM 
(NH4)2SO4]. The OD was adjusted to 0.2 using the chemotaxis buffer, and 200µl of cells were added 
to each well of a 96-well plate. Capillaries (Microcaps 1µl, Drummond) were carefully placed into a 
micropipette and 1µl of the root exudates of either A. thaliana or Tomato was taken up. Afterwards, 
capillaries were dipped in the bacterial solution for 1h at room temperature. Capillaries were then 
picked up and rinsed in sterile MQ, to remove bacterial cells attaching to the outer surface of the 
capillary. The capillary´s content was squeezed out into 1ml of 0.9% NaCl using the micropipette, 
diluted 10 to 100-fold in 0.9% NaCl and plated on LB-agar. Plates were incubated at 30°C for 14-16h 
and CFU were counted. Four replicates of three isolates evolved only on A. thaliana, and one isolate 
evolved only on Tomato, as well as of the ancestor, were tested for a chemotactic response towards 
root exudates of A. thaliana and Tomato, as well as one replicate for water as negative control (Supp. 
1). 
4.5.5. Growth rate  
In order to investigate, if evolved isolates improved in utilizing plant polysaccharides in comparison to 
the ancestor, the growth of evolved isolates was tested in MSNc + 0,5% xylan. Therefore, ON cultures 
were prepared and the OD was adjusted to 0.1 in MSNc + 0.5% xylan. Into each well of a 96-well plate, 
100µl of the bacterial suspension was transferred. The plates were incubated in a plate reader for 58h 
at 24°C and the OD was measured every 15 minutes in a 9-point measurement at different points of 
each well, to exclude errors of clumping and inhomogeneous suspensions. Overall, two ON of 12 
evolved isolates, including 7 isolates, which only evolved on A. thaliana, and 5 isolates, evolved only 
on Tomato, as well as of the ancestor were tested in triplicates (Supp. 1 ). In addition, a blank medium 
control was included. 
==PAGE:33==
20 
 
4.6. 
Competition in root colonization of isolates evolved on distinct plant species 
In order to investigate, if B. subtilis adapted specifically in root colonization towards the plant species 
it evolved on, an isolate that evolved on A. thaliana (1E_3) and an isolate evolved on Tomato (2A_1) 
were tested in direct competition in root colonization on both plant species. In addition, both isolates 
were tested in direct competition against the ancestor as a control. Therefore, ON cultures of each 
strain labeled with either GFP or mKATE were prepared and regrown until exponential phase. The OD 
was adjusted to 0.2 in MSNg and 1:1 mixes of contrary fluorescent-labeled strains were prepared. A 
part of the mix was diluted in 0.9% NaCl and plated on LB-agar, to allow for CFU counting and 
determine the exact starting ratios. Both, A. thaliana and Tomato seedlings, were inoculated with the 
1:1 mixes as previously described for the experimental evolution (see 4.3.). After 48h of incubation in 
the plant chamber, roots were washed in sterile MSNg, transferred to 2ml Eppendorf tubes containing 
1ml 0.9% NaCl and vortexed with small glass beads. The bacterial suspension was diluted in 0.9% NaCl, 
plated on LB-Agar and incubated for 14-16h at 30°C. GFP and mKATE labeled colonies were 
differentiated by the color of the colonies and CFU for the different strains of the mix were counted. 
Four replicates per fluorescent-labeled combinations were tested on both plant species. The number 
of cells colonizing the roots was normalized for each isolate by dividing with its starting CFU in the 1:1 
mix. The ratio of colonization capacity was calculated by dividing the normalized CFU of each isolate 
of the 1:1 mix with the sum of both normalized CFU´s. 
4.7. 
Root colonization of mixed populations 
In order to investigate, if and how different morphotypes interact when colonizing a plant root 
together, A. thaliana seedlings were inoculated with three different 1:1:1 mixtures, including two 
mixes with three different morphotypes of the same lineage (Lin1E) (SN:SM:WR and SN:SM:WRS), as 
well as one mix with two distinct morphotypes of the same lineage (Lin1E) together with the ancestor 
(SN:SM:A). Therefore, ON cultures of the different strains were prepared and regrown until 
exponential phase. Afterwards, the OD of the different cultures was adjusted to 0.2 in MSNg and 1:1:1 
mixes were prepared. A. thaliana seedlings were inoculated following the same protocol as previously 
described for the evolution experiment (see 4.3.) with both the 1:1:1 mixes as well as all strains in 
monoculture. After 48h of incubation, the roots were washed in sterile MSNg and vortexed with small 
glass beads. Bacterial suspensions were plated on LB-agar and incubated for 14-16h at 30°C. 
Afterwards, the CFU of each morphotype was counted. Finally, an expected colonization value was 
calculated as the product of the proportion of each morphotype in the inoculant (1/3) and its 
colonization (CFU per root) in monoculture (Loreau & Hector 2001; Poltak & Cooper 2011). Two 
biological replicates were prepared for two of the mixes (SN:SM:WR and SN:SM:A), while for the third 
mix three (SN:SM:WRS) biological replicates were prepared, each in four technical replicates.  
==PAGE:34==
21 
 
4.8. 
DNA extraction and re-sequencing of evolved isolates 
To analyze, which genetic mutations occurred in the course of evolution of B. subtilis on plant root 
colonization, twelve evolved isolates were chosen to be re-sequence based on their evolved 
properties, including changes in morphology, biofilm formation and root colonization. Of the twelve 
chosen isolates, seven evolved only on A. thaliana (1A_1, 1A_2, 1D_1, 1D_2, 1E_1, 1E_2 and 1E_3) 
and five only on Tomato (2A_1, 2B_2, 2B_3, 2C_1 and 2C_3). ON cultures of the evolved isolates were 
prepared and genomic DNA was extracted using a standard method and DNA purification kit 
(GeneMATRIX Bacterial & Yeast GenomicDNA Purification Kit, EurX). Samples were sent for next-
generation sequencing to a collaborating laboratory (SeqOmics Biotechnology Ltd, Hungary). Re-
sequencing is still in progress and results will therefore not be further discussed in this thesis. 
4.9. 
Studying Δhag mutant compared to the WT 
4.9.1. Competition assay  
In order to investigate, if motility is necessary for plant root (re-) colonization, B. subtilis WT and a 
non-motile ∆hag mutant were studied in direct competition. Therefore, ON cultures were prepared 
for GFP and mKATE labeled WT and Δhag mutants. To ensure that all cells were in the same growth 
state bacterial cultures were diluted 100-fold on the next morning and were re-grown at 37°C for 3-
4h. The OD of the cultures was adjusted to 0.2. and 1:1 mixes of contrary fluorescent-labeled WT and 
Δhag cultures were prepared in 1.5ml Eppendorf tubes. Furthermore, to test if the fluorescent marker 
has any influence on the performance of the bacterial strain, a 1:1 mix of contrary fluorescent-labeled 
WT cultures as well as a 1:1 mix of contrary fluorescent-labeled mutant cultures was prepared. 
For this set-up homogenous 6-8 days old A. thaliana seedlings of 0.8 – 1.2cm length were used. The 
seedlings were suspended in 270µl MSNg medium in a 48-well plate, and each well was inoculated 
with 30µl of the mixed bacterial culture, reaching a starting OD of 0.02. Plates were incubated in the 
plant chamber for 48h either under shaking at 200rpm or static conditions. Afterwards, the seedlings 
were washed in MSNg medium and transferred to a new well containing 300µl fresh MSNg medium 
and a new sterile seedling. They were incubated again for 48h in the plant chamber and this step was 
repeated once more. The third seedling was washed and vortexed with glass beads to disrupt the 
biofilm on the roots as previously described (see 4.3. ), however smaller glass beads have been used 
(Ø0.25-0..5mm, Roth). Afterwards, a dilution series was prepared in 0.9% NaCl and plated on LB-Agar. 
The LB-plates were incubated at 37°C for 14-16h and CFU were counted. Overall, five replicates per 
fluorescent-labeled combination were prepared.  
4.9.2. Fluorescence microscopy 
To further verify, that motility is essential for plant root (re-)colonization, the competition in root 
colonization of B. subtilis WT and a non-motile Δhag mutant was analyzed under a fluorescence 
==PAGE:35==
22 
 
stereomicroscope. Therefore, a 1:1 mix with a GFP-labeled WT and an mKATE-labeled mutant was 
prepared as previously described and the OD was adjusted to 0.2 in MSNg. Afterwards, a 6d old A. 
thaliana seedling of around 1cm length was transferred to a small Petri dish and 1.8ml of MSNg 
medium was added. The Petri dish was inoculated with 0.2ml of the bacterial mix reaching a starting 
OD of 0.02 and incubated in a plant chamber for 48h shaking at 200rpm. Afterwards, a new Petri dish 
was prepared by adding 0.5% MSNg Agar to the bottom of the dish and transferring a fresh sterile 
seedling on top. The previously colonized seedling was carefully placed next to the sterile seedling and 
a small glass beat was placed on top of the leaves to fix the seedlings on the plate. Afterwards, 1ml of 
MSNg medium was carefully added on top. The Petri dish was placed inside the microscope chamber 
with 22°C bottom heat, 24°C water bath heat and 26°C top heat. Pictures were taken every 30min for 
50h under brightfield with 5000ms exposure time, as well as red and green fluorescence with 10000ms 
exposure time at ten different focus stacks. Pictures were analyzed with ImageJ and a time-lapse 
movie was created. 
4.10. Statistical analysis  
The statistical analysis was performed with Origin (2019b) and Rstudio (R version i386 3.6.1.). Possible 
outliers were identified using Grubbs´s test in Origin. The mean of the ratios of the competition 
between Δhag and WT as well as for the competition between isolates evolved on distinct plant 
species and the ancestor were tested for a significant difference compared to a test mean of 1 using 
One-Sample t-test in Origin. CFU during the experimental evolution, pellicle dry and fresh weight of 
distinct morphotypes, as well as root colonization, biofilm formation and swimming and swarming 
motility were tested for significance using Dunnett´s test with the ancestor as reference value in 
Rstudio. 
All figures were constructed in Origin.  
Furthermore, the following equations have been used in this study:

5. Results 
5.1. 
Competition between B. subtilis WT and Δhag mutant 
Motility is an essential trait for bacteria to be able to reach and colonize plant roots (Allard-Massicotte 
et al., 2016). To test the role of motility in root colonization and re-colonization of B. subtilis on A. 
thaliana, a Δhag mutant, deficient in the production of the flagellin protein, was studied in direct 
competition with the WT. This was carried out under either static conditions, were motility is required 
for cells to reach the root, or shaken conditions, where the impact of motility is supposed to be 
reduced. In this experiment, 6-8d old sterile A. thaliana seedlings were initially inoculated with a 1:1 
mix of the WT and Δhag mutant, labeled with contrary fluorescence tags, and incubated for 48h. The 
colonized seedlings were subsequently transferred twice to new sterile seedlings. The third root was 
vortexed to disperse the biofilm and plated on LB-agar to allow CFU counting. Furthermore, 
competition experiments for root colonization between contrary fluorescence-tagged WT strains as 
well as the contrary fluorescence-tagged Δhag mutant were set up as control (Supp. 2).  
Under shaken conditions, the ratio between the WT and Δhag mutant did not change after three 
transfers, compared to the 1:1 inoculation ratio (Fig. 6). The ratio of cells colonizing the third root was 
on average around 56.7% ± 6.7% WT cells and 43.3% ± 6.7 Δhag cells. Under static conditions, the WT 
outcompeted the Δhag mutant and the ratio of colonizing cells on the third root was on average 
around 89.3% ± 3.1% WT cells and 10.7% ± 3.1% Δhag cells (p<0.001) (Fig. 6). 
Fig. 6: Competition for root re-colonization between ∆hag and WT.  6-8 days old A. thaliana seedlings 
were inoculated with 1:1 mixed ∆hag mutant and WT B. subtilis, incubated for 48h in microtiter plates 
either shaking at 200 rpm or not shaking. Colonized roots were consecutively transferred twice to a new 
sterile root and incubated under the same conditions. The third root was vortexed to disperse the biofilm 
and the suspension was plated on LB-agar to allow CFU counting. Bars represent the ratio of ∆hag cells 
(light blue) and WT cells (orange) on the final root. Error bars represent the standard error for ten 
replicates (N=10) under shaken and nine replicates (N=9) under non-shaken conditions. The mean of the 
ratios of ∆hag:WT cells was tested for a significant difference to a mean value of 1 with One-sample t-Test 
(*** : p<0.001) A significant outlier was identified with Grubbs test in the Non-shaken set-up and removed. 
*** 
==PAGE:38==
25 
 
To confirm the importance of motility in a static system, a time-lapse movie capturing the competition 
in root re-colonization between the WT and Δhag mutant was constructed using fluorescence 
microscopy (Supp. 3, or click here). To this end, a 4-6d old A. thaliana seedling was inoculated with a 
1:1 mix of GFP-labeled WT and mKATE-labeled Δhag mutant, incubated for 48h in the plant chamber 
while shaking, and transferred to a small Petri dish containing a new sterile seedling and fresh media. 
The Petri dish was placed under a stereomicroscope under static conditions in a heated chamber at 
24°C and pictures were taken every 10 minutes. 
After around 35h a green fluorescent signal was observed on the tip of the new root (left root) and 
after 48h the whole root was colonized by the green fluorescent WT, while nearly no red fluorescent 
signal was detected (Fig. 7). This confirms, that the WT indeed outcompetes the Δhag mutant in root 
re-colonization on A. thaliana under static conditions. 
Fig. 7: Fluorescence microscopy of plant root re-colonization of B. subtilis ∆hag mutant vs. WT. A 6-8 days 
old A. thaliana seedling was inoculated with 1:1 mixed mKATE-labeled ∆hag mutant and GFP-labeled WT, and 
incubated under shaking conditions for 48h in a small Petri dish. Afterwards, the colonized root (right root) was 
transferred to a new Petri dish containing new medium and a sterile root (left root). Pictures were taken using 
a fluorescence microscope every 30 minutes for 50 hours under static conditions. Brightness and contrast of 
pictures were adjusted using ImageJ. 
==PAGE:39==
26 
 
5.2. 
Population development during experimental evolution 
To examine how B. subtilis evolves during root colonization of two distinct plant species, experimental 
evolution of B. subtilis 3610 was performed on either A. thaliana, Tomato or alternating between the 
two species for a total of 20 transfers. Therefore, either 14-16d old A. thaliana or 8-10d old Tomato 
seedlings were inoculated with B. subtilis with an OD of 0.02 in MSGg medium, incubated for 48h 
under static conditions in a plant chamber and consecutively transferred to a new sterile root. The 
number of cells that colonized the root was quantified by CFU counting for root 1, 5, 10, 17 and 20 
and a frozen stock was prepared for later comparison.  
5.2.1.  CFU during experimental evolution 
In all four evolution experiments, the number of cells colonizing the root increased significantly during 
the evolution, with an increase of approx. 10-fold or more already after five transfers (Fig. 8). After 
the fifth transfer, the CFU varied over time but overall no considerable change was observed in either 
of the four evolution experiments (Fig. 8). 
In the mono evolution experiment, where B. subtilis evolved only on A. thaliana, the number of cells 
colonizing the roots did, on average for the five lineages, show a significant increase of approx. 9.1-
fold (p<0.01) (Fig. 8a) (Significance is not shown in the figure). The number of bacteria colonizing roots 
in the evolution experiment on Tomato did on average increase approx. 7.5-fold (p<0.01) (Fig. 8b). 
The number of cells colonizing the roots in the alternating evolution experiment starting A. thaliana 
increased approx. 25.3-fold (p<0.001) (Fig. 8c), while for the evolution experiment starting with 
Tomato an approx. 12.0-fold increase was observed (p<0.001) (Fig. 8d).  
 
==PAGE:40==
27 
 
 
5.2.2. Morphological diversification of populations during evolution 
During the experimental evolution of B. subtilis on plant roots, the arise and coexistence of distinct 
morphotypes was observed in all evolution experiments (Fig. 9). Re-streaking of different 
morphotypes confirmed that they were genetically stable. In total three distinct morphology types 
were observed throughout all evolution experiments, termed Wrinkle (WR), Snow (SN) and Smooth 
(SM).  
Fig. 8: Development of CFU per gram root during the evolution experiments. Either 14-16d old A. thaliana or 8-10d 
old Tomato seedlings were inoculated with B. subtilis with a starting OD adjusted to 0.02, incubated for 48h under 
static conditions in a plant chamber and consecutively transferred to a new sterile root. Roots were vortexed with 
small glass beads after transfer 1, 5, 10, 17 and 20 to disrupt the biofilm and the suspension was plated on LB-agar to 
allow CFU counting. In evolution experiment 1 (A) B. subtilis was evolved only on A. thaliana; in evolution experiment 
2 (B) B. subtilis was evolved only on Tomato; in evolution experiment 3 (C) B. subtilis was initially inoculated on A. 
thaliana and the plant species was switched after every third transfer; in evolution experiment 4 (D) B. subtilis was 
initially inoculated on Tomato and the plant species was switched after every third transfer. The grey lines represent 
the five different lineages. The thick black line represents the average of all five lineages.  
==PAGE:41==
28 
 
The different morphotypes were defined by their colony appearance when plated on LB-agar, with a 
high density of colonies (Fig. 9, third row). A WR variant was defined by a wrinkled colony, which is 
not differentiable from the ancestral strain when plated in high density on LB-agar (Fig. 9B). However, 
a subtype of the WR variant, termed Wrinkle(Sharp) (WRS) was observed when spotting 2µl of the ON 
culture of a WR variant on LB-agar and was defined by a wrinkled colony morphology with a prominent 
sharp ring around the edge (Fig. 9B, second row). An SN variant was defined by bigger, highly wrinkled 
and whitish colonies on LB-agar compared to the ancestor (Fig. 9C). An SM type was observed to form 
smaller colonies on LB-agar with a smooth surface and edge (Fig. 9D).  
In order to investigate, how the distribution of the distinct morphotypes developed during the 
experimental evolution, ON cultures of the frozen stocks of evolved populations from root 5, 10, 17 
and 20 as well as from the ancestral strain (“transfer 0”) were prepared and plated on LB-agar. WR, 
SN and SM variants were identified and counted to calculate the ratio of a given morphotype at each 
time point for each lineage.  
Because all lineages were inoculated by the same ancestral strain, only WR variants were observed at 
the beginning of the evolution (“transfer 0”) (Fig. 10). At transfer 5, the SN variant was observed in all 
lineages, while at transfer 10 the SM variant was observed in 11 of the 20 lineages (Fig. 10).  
At transfer 5 of the evolution experiment, in which B. subtilis evolved only on A. thaliana, the WR 
variant made up 78.2% ± 0.8% of the population, on average over all 5 lineages, while the SN variant 
made up the remaining 21.8% ± 0.8% (Fig. 10a). At transfer 10, WR contributed to 86.8% ± 7%, while  
SN decreased to 8.6% ± 2.7% and SM appeared for the first time at 4.5% ± 1.5% (Fig. 10a). At transfer 
17, the population consisted of 86.1% ± 2.7% WR, 9.0% ± 2.6% SN and 4.9% ± 0.4% SM cells (Fig. 10a). 
At the end of the evolution, WR and SN were observed in all five lineages, while SM was present in 3 
 Wrinkle(Sharp) (WRS) 
Fig. 9: Three distinct morphology types (Wrinkle, Snow and Smooth) were observed in evolved populations of B. subtilis 
after experimental evolution on plant roots. To analyze how B. subtilis diversified during the experimental evolution, ON 
cultures were prepared from frozen stocks of evolved populations from root 20 of all lineages and plated on LB. Three distinct 
colony morphologies were identified (Wrinkle, Snow and Smooth) in all evolution experiments. ON cultures were prepared 
from a few randomly chosen isolates of the three morphotypes. They were plated in high density on LB-agar and additionally 
2µl of the ON culture was spotted on LB-Agar in 9 replicates. Agar plates were incubated for 48h at 30°C and images were 
acquired using a stereomicroscope.   
==PAGE:42==
29 
 
of the 5 lineages. In average over all five lineages, WR contributed to 80.0% ± 2.6%, SN to 17.3% ± 
1.8% and SM to 2.7% ± 1.3% of the whole population (Fig. 10a).  
At transfer 5 of the evolution experiment, in which B. subtilis evolved only on Tomato, WR contributed 
to 84.5% ± 1.4% of the population, while SN made up the remaining 15.5% ± 1.4% (Fig. 10b). At transfer 
10,  94.6% ± 2.6% of the population was WR, while SN decreased to 0.1% ± 0.1% and SM was observed 
for the first time at 5.2% ± 2.5% (Fig. 10b).  At transfer 17, WR made up 81.1% ± 5.4% of the population, 
while SN contributed to 0.8% ± 0.6% and SM to 18.1% ± 5.1% (Fig. 10b). At the final transfer 20,  all 
three variants were observed in all lineages. The population consisted on average of 80.4% ± 3.4% 
WR, 15.3% ± 2.6% SN and 4.4% ± 1.1% SM cells (Fig. 10b).  
In the cycling evolution experiment, starting with A. thaliana, WR made up 76.2% ±  0.7% of the 
population at transfer 5, while SN contributed the remaining 23.8% ± 0.7% (Fig. 10c). At transfer 10, 
the population consisted of 90.4% ± 5.2% WR, 5.7% ± 3.5% SN and 3.9% ± 3.5% SM cells (Fig. 10c). At 
transfer 17, WR contributed to 90.0% ± 3.8%, SN to 0.8 ± 0.5% and SM to 8.2% ± 3.3% of the whole 
population (Fig. 10c). At the end of the evolution, all three morphotypes were observed in all five 
lineages. The population consisted on average of 74.6% ± 4.0% WR, 22.7% ± 3.9% SN and 2.6% ± 1% 
SM cells (Fig. 10c). 
At transfer 5 of the cycling evolution experiment starting with Tomato, the population consisted of 
78.7% ± 2.3% WR and 21.3% ± 2.3% SN cells (Fig. 10d). At transfer 10, WR made up 73.6% ± 14.2% of 
the population, while SN contributed to 19.8% ± 11.0% and SM to 6.6% ± 3.2% (Fig. 10d). At transfer 
17, WR contributed to 72.3% ±  14.2%, SN to 26.0% ± 14.6% and SM to 1.7% ± 0.8% of the whole 
population (Fig. 10d). At the final transfer 20, all three morphotypes were observed in all five lineages. 
The population consisted on average of 80.7% ± 2.4% WR,  16.7% ± 1.8% SN and 2.6% ± 1% SM cells 
(Fig. 10d).  
 
==PAGE:43==

5.2.3. Biofilm capacity of distinct morphotypes 
In order to investigate further phenotypical changes of the three distinct morphological variants, 
biofilm capacity was tested for random isolates of transfer 20. Therefore, pellicle formation was tested 
in LB + 0.5% xylan in 6-well plates for the ancestor, two WR (2A_1 and 2B_2, evolved on Tomato), two 
SN (1A_1 and 1E_1, evolved on A. thaliana) and two SM (1D_1 and 1E_2, evolved on A. thaliana). After 
48h of incubation at 30°C, images were taken with a camera, the pellicle was carefully harvested and 
fresh and dry weight was measured.  
Fig. 10: Diversification of B. subtilis populations into three distinct morphotypes (Wrinkle, Snow and Smooth) over time 
during experimental evolution on plants. To analyze how B. subtilis diversified during experimental evolution in plant root 
colonization over time, ON cultures were prepared from frozen stocks of evolved populations from root 5, 10, 17 and 20 of 
all lineages as well as of the ancestral strain used for the experimental evolution (“transfer 0”)  and plated on LB-agar. Plates
were incubated for 14-16h at 30°C, and WR, SM and SN variants were identified and counted to calculate the ratio of a given 
morphotype at each time point for each lineage. Shaded areas describe the mean ratio of all five lineages for (A) Assay 1 
(evolved only on A. thaliana); (B) Assay 2 (evolved only on Tomato); (C) Assay 3 (Evolved alternating on A. thaliana and 
Tomato) and (D) Assay 4 (evolved alternating on Tomato and A. thaliana) for a given morphotype of the whole population in 
grey for WR, light blue for SM and orange for SN. Error bars represent the standard error of the five lineages.  
==PAGE:44==
31 
 
Compared to the ancestor the SN morphotype shows a more complex wrinkled and whitish pellicle, 
while SM was not able to form a stable pellicle, but only a thin layer on the air-liquid interface (Fig. 
11c). The pellicle of WR looks similar to the ancestor, with slightly smaller but more wrinkles (Fig. 11c). 
The biomass of SN pellicles is significantly increased compared to the ancestral strain for approx. 1.6-
fold in fresh weight (p<0.001) (Fig. 11a) and approx. 1.2-fold in dry weight (p<0.001) (Fig 11b), while 
no significant change was observed for WR. Pellicles formed by SM were not stable enough and could 
not be harvested for further analysis.  
5.3. 
Evolved Isolates 
To analyze potential phenotypical changes of evolved bacteria, 12 isolates from four different lineages 
evolved only on A. thaliana, as well as nine isolates from three different lineages evolved only on 
Tomato, were randomly chosen from the populations after the last transfer (Root 20). They were 
tested for changes in morphology, biofilm formation, motility, growth and root colonization.  
 
Fig. 11 Pellicle capacity in response to plant polysaccharides of the evolved morphotypes SN, SM and WR in comparison to 
the ancestor. Pellicle capacity of the evolved morphotypes SN, SM and WR as well as of the ancestor was tested by inoculating 
LB + 0.5% xylan with ON cultures, which were adjusted to a final OD of 0.05, in 6-well microtiter plates. Plates were incubated 
at 30°C for 48h under static conditions, to allow pellicle formation. Images of the pellicles were taken (C), followed by 
harvesting of the pellicles using an inoculation loop and transferred to a 1.5ml Eppendorf tube. The pellicles were centrifuged 
to remove remaining liquid and to measure the fresh weight (A). Afterwards, pellicles were dried for 72h at 60°C and the dry 
weight was measured (B). The relative biomass was calculated by dividing the weight of each replicate pellicle of the tested 
isolates with the average weight of the ancestor pellicles. Data points represent three replicates of two isolates of the same 
morphotypes or six replicates of the ancestor. Dunnett´s test was performed to test for significance between each 
morphotype and the ancestor (***: p<0.001) 
==PAGE:45==
32 
 
5.3.1. Morphology 
To analyze differences in colony morphology, three randomly chosen isolates from each of four 
different lineages that evolved only on A. thaliana and of three lineages, which evolved only on 
Tomato, were spotted on LB-agar and images were acquired under a stereo-microscope. Because 
images of different lineages were acquired on multiple days, the ancestor was included in each 
experimental set-up for comparison.  
 Of the twelve isolates, which evolved only on A. thaliana, five showed a WR colony type (1A_2, 1A_3, 
1C_3, 1D_2 and 1D_3), one exhibited WRS (1E_3), while four isolates showed an SN morphology 
(1A_1, 1C_1, 1C_2 and 1E_1) and two were an SM variant (1D_1 and 1E_2) (Fig. 12).  
 
 
Fig. 12: Colony morphology of isolates evolved only on A. thaliana compared to the ancestor on LB-agar. To analyze the 
colony morphology of the evolved isolates, 2µl of an ON cultures of the evolved isolates (1A_1, 1A_2, 1A_3, 1C_1, 1C_2, 
1C_3, 1D_1, 1D_2, 1D_3, 1E_1, 1E_2 and 1E_3) as well as of the ancestor were spotted on LB-agar and incubated for 48h at 
30°C. Images were taken with a stereomicroscope set at 5X magnification.   
==PAGE:46==
33 
 
Of the nine isolates, which evolved only on Tomato, five showed WR colony types (2A_3, 2B_1, 2B_3, 
2C_1 and 2C_3), while the other four were WRS (2A_1, 2A_2, 2B_2 and 2C_2) (Fig. 13).  
5.3.2. Plant root colonization 
In order to test, if B. subtilis improved in root colonization during the evolution and whether those 
changes confer only to the plant species they evolved on, or to both plant species, both 14-16 days 
old A. thaliana and 10-12 days old Tomato seedlings were inoculated with the isolates, which have 
been previously tested for colony morphology. They were incubated for 48h under static conditions 
in a plant chamber and the number of cells that colonized the root was quantified by CFU counting. 
Because different lineages were tested on multiple days, the ancestor was included in each 
experimental set-up for comparison.  
Overall, from the twelve isolates that evolved only on A. thaliana, eight showed significantly improved 
root colonization on A. thaliana (1A_1, 1A_2, 1C_1, 1C_2, 1D_2, 1D_3, 1E_1 and 1E_3), while none 
exhibited significantly improved colonization on Tomato (Fig. 14).  
Compared to the ancestral strain, the highest improvement in root colonization on A. thaliana from 
isolates that evolved only on A. thaliana was found for the SN isolate 1A_1, which showed a significant 
approx. 35-fold increase on A. thaliana (p<0.01) (Fig. 14). The other SN isolates, 1C_1, 1C_2 and 1E_1 
also showed significant improvements in root colonization on A. thaliana (p<0.05), with an approx. 
12-fold, 17-fold and 3-fold increase in CFU, respectively (Fig. 14). Furthermore, the SN isolate 1E_1 
Fig. 13: Colony morphology of isolates evolved only on Tomato compared to the ancestor on LB-agar. To analyze the colony 
morphology of the evolved isolates, 2µl of an ON cultures of the evolved isolates (2A_1, 2A_2, 2A_3, 2B_1, 2B_2, 2B_3, 2C_1, 
2C_2 and 2C_3) as well as of the ancestor were spotted on LB-agar and incubated for 48h at 30°C. Pictures were taken with 
a stereomicroscope set at 5X magnification.   
==PAGE:47==
34 
 
exhibited a significant ~3-fold decrease on Tomato (p<0.01) (Fig. 14). The WR isolates 1A_2, 1D_2 and 
1D_3 were all significantly enhanced in root colonization on A. thaliana (p<0.05, p<0.001 and p<0.05, 
respectively), with an approx. 22-fold, 28-fold and 7-fold increase in cells on the roots, respectively 
(Fig. 14). On the contrary, no significant change was observed in the number of cells colonizing the 
roots for the WR isolates 1A_3 and 1C_3 on either of the two plant species (Fig. 14). The WRS isolate 
1E_3 exhibited a significant ~13-fold in root colonization on A. thaliana (p<0.001), (Fig. 14). The only 
isolate that was significantly reduced in root colonization on both plant species was the SM isolate 
1E_2, showing an approx. 6-fold decrease on A. thaliana (p<0.05) and 5-fold on Tomato (p<0.001) (Fig. 
14). No significant change was observed in root colonization on either plant species for the other SM 
isolate 1D_1 (Fig. 14).   
Fig. 14: Root colonization of evolved isolates, which evolved only on A. thaliana, compared to the ancestor. Both 14-16 
days old A. thaliana (blue) and 10-12 days old Tomato seedlings (orange) were inoculated with different evolved isolates 
(1A_1, 1A_2, 1A_3, 1C_1, 1C_2, 1C_3, 1D_1, 1D_2, 1D_3, 1E_1, 1E_2 and 1E_3) of assay 1 (evolved only on A. thaliana)  as 
well as the ancestor and incubated for 48h in a plant chamber. The biofilm on the roots was disrupted through vortexing 
with small glass beads and the suspension was plated on LB-agar to allow CFU counting. Relative root colonization was 
calculated, by dividing the log10-transformed CFU of each isolate with the mean of the log10-transformed CFU of the 
ancestor. The dashed line represents the mean of the ancestor. Dunnett´s test was performed to test for significance 
between each isolate and the ancestor (* : p<0.05, **: p<0.01, ***: p<0.001) 
==PAGE:48==
35 
 
From the nine isolates, that evolved only on Tomato, one showed significantly improved root 
colonization on Tomato (2A_1), while two isolates exhibited significantly improved root colonization 
on A. thaliana (2C_1 and 2C_3) (Fig. 15).  
The WRS isolate 2A_1 was the only isolate, which improved significantly (~4-fold) in colonizing Tomato 
(p<0.01), while no change was observed when colonizing A. thaliana (Fig. 15). The WR isolate 2C_1 
showed a significant ~24-fold increase in colonizing A. thaliana (p<0.01), while no change was found 
on Tomato (Fig. 15). The WR isolate 2C_3 exhibited a significant ~33-fold increase in root colonization 
on A. thaliana (p<0.01), while it also showed a trend in improved root colonization on Tomato (~4-
fold), even though it is not significant (p=0.0636) (Fig. 15).  
Fig. 15: Root colonization of evolved isolates, which evolved only on Tomato, compared to the ancestor. Both 
14-16 days old A. thaliana (blue) and 8-10d old Tomato seedlings (orange) were inoculated with different 
evolved isolates (2A_1, 2A_2, 2A_3, 2B_1, 2B_2, 2B_3, 2C_1, 2C_2 and 2C_3) of assay 2 (evolved only on Tomato) 
as well as the ancestor and incubated for 48h in a plant chamber. The biofilm on the roots was disrupted through 
vortexing with small glass beads and the suspension was plated on LB-agar to allow CFU counting. Because 
different lineages were tested on multiple days, the ancestor was included in every experimental set-up for 
comparison. Relative root colonization was calculated by dividing the log10-transformed CFU per gram root of 
each replicate of the isolates with the mean of the log10-transformed CFU per gram root of the ancestor of the 
same experimental set-up. The mean of the ancestor was set to 1 and is represented by a dashed line. Dunnett´s 
test was performed to test for significance between each isolate and the ancestor (**: p<0.01) 
==PAGE:49==
36 
 
The WR isolate 2C_2 showed a trend in improved root colonization on Tomato (~ 4-fold), even though 
it is not significant (p=0.0768), while no change was found on A. thaliana (Fig. 15). No significant 
change on either plant species was observed for the WR 2A_3, 2B_1 and 2B_3 isolates as well as the 
isolates WRS 2A_2 and 2B_2 (Fig. 15).  
5.3.3. Biofilm formation 
The ability to form a stable biofilm seems to be highly advantageous for successful root colonization 
(Chen et al. 2013). In order to investigate, if the biofilm capacity of B. subtilis improved in response to 
plant polysaccharides during the evolution experiment, evolved isolates were tested for pellicle 
formation in LB + 0.5% xylan. After 48h of incubation at 30°C, pictures were taken under a 
stereomicroscope and pellicles were carefully harvested and mildly sonicated to allow for counting of 
CFU.  
Most of the evolved isolates showed at least minor changes in pellicle complexity on microscopy 
images. However, no significant improvements in pellicle productivity, as seen in the number of cells 
forming the pellicle, were observed for any of the evolved isolates. On the contrary, three of the 21 
tested isolates showed significantly reduced pellicle CFU.  
Of the 12 isolates, which evolved only on A. thaliana, especially the SN isolates 1A_1, 1C_1, 1C_2 and 
1E_1 showed thicker and more complex, i.e. more wrinkled, pellicle formation (Fig. 16). The SM 
isolates 1D_1 and 1E_2 did not form stable pellicles, but just a thin film on the air-liquid interface (Fig 
16). The WR isolates 1A_2, 1A_3, 1D_2 and 1D_3 showed slightly more structured pellicles, while no 
difference in pellicle formation was observed for the WR isolate 1C_3 and the WRS isolate 1E_3 (Fig. 
16). 
 
==PAGE:50==
37 
 
 
Fig. 16: Pellicle formation of isolates, which evolved only on A. thaliana, compared to the ancestral strain in LB + 0.5% 
xylan. Evolved isolates (1A_1, 1A_2, 1A_3, 1C_1, 1C_2, 1C_3, 1D_1, 1D_2, 1D_3, 1E_1, 1E_2 and 1E_3) were tested for
improved pellicle formation compared to the ancestor by inoculating LB + 0.5% xylan with ON cultures, which were adjusted 
to a final OD of 0.05, in 24-well microtiter plates. Plates were incubated for 48h at 30°C under static conditions to allow 
pellicle formation. Pictures were taken under the stereomicroscope set at 3.2× magnification.  
==PAGE:51==

Eight of the nine isolates, which evolved only on Tomato, showed at least minor changes in pellicle 
complexity. The WRS 2A_1, 2A_2, 2B_2 and 2C_2 as well as the WR 2B1, 2B_3 and 2C_3 showed 
slightly more wrinkled pellicles, while the WR 2A_3 showed reduced pellicle complexity (Fig. 17). No 
change in pellicle complexity was observed for the WR isolate 2C_1 (Fig 17).  
Improvements in complexity and structure did not confer with an increase in the number of cells in 
pellicles. None of the 10 isolates, which evolved on A. thaliana, as well as none of the 9 isolates, that 
evolved on Tomato, showed a significant increase in the number of cells forming the pellicle. The SM 
isolates 1D_1 and 1E_2 were not stable enough to be harvested and were therefore not tested for 
pellicle CFU.  
The WR isolates 1D_2 and 1D_3 showed a trend towards an increased number of cells in pellicles, 
however, it was not significant (p=0.0752 and p=0.0652, respectively) (Fig. 18). The WR isolates 1A_3, 
1C_3 and 2B_1 showed significantly reduced pellicle CFU (p<0.01, p<0.01 and p<0.05, respectively), 
Fig. 17: Pellicle formation of isolates, which evolved only on Tomato, compared to the ancestral strain in LB + 0.5% xylan. 
Evolved isolates (2A_1, 2A_2, 2A_3, 2B_1, 2B_2, 2B_3, 2C_1, 2C_2 and 2C_3) were tested for improved pellicle formation

while no change was observed for the other tested isolates (1A_1, 1A_2, 1C_1, 1C_2, 2A_1, 2A_2, 
2A_3, 2B_1, 2B_2, 2B_3, 2C_1, 2C_2 and 2C_3) (Fig. 18). 
5.3.4. Motility 
Motility, especially in the form of chemotaxis, seems to be decisive for bacterial cells to reach and 
colonize plant roots (Allard-Massicotte et al. 2016). In order to examine, if B. subtilis improved in 
motility during the evolution experiment, evolved isolates were tested for swimming and swarming 
motility, by spotting 2µl of an ON culture on 0.3% and 0.7% LB-agar, respectively. Furthermore, the 
chemotactic response towards root exudates was measured in capillary assays. However, the 
chemotactic assay showed great variation and was inconclusive, because all isolates, as well as the 
Fig. 18: Relative pellicle productivity of evolved isolates compared to the ancestor. Evolved isolates (1A_1, 1A_2, 1A_3, 
1C_1, 1C_2, 1C_3, 1D_1, 1D_2, 1D_3, 1E_1, 1E_2,1E_3, 2A_1, 2A_2, 2A_3, 2B_1, 2B_2, 2B_3, 2C_1, 2C_2 and 2C_3) 
were tested for improved biofilm productivity compared to the ancestor by inoculating LB + 0.5% xylan with ON cultures, 
which were adjusted to a final OD of 0.05, in 24-well microtiter plates. Plates were incubated for 48h at 30°C under
static conditions to allow pellicle formation. Afterwards, the pellicle was harvested with an inoculation loop, transfer to 
an Eppendorf tube and mildly sonicated to disrupt the biofilm. The bacterial suspensions were plated on LB-agar to 
allow CFU counting. Relative biofilm productivity was calculated by dividing the log10-transformed CFU of each replicate 
of the isolates by the mean of the log10-transformed CFU of the ancestor. Dunnett´s test was performed to test for 
significance between each isolate and the ancestor (* : p<0.05, **: p<0.01). 
** 
** 
* 
==PAGE:53==

ancestral strain, showed a similar response to the water control, compared to the root exudates (Supp. 
21). 
None of the seven isolates, which evolved on A. thaliana, as well as none of the five isolates, that 
evolved on Tomato, showed improvements in either swimming or swarming. On the contrary, most 
isolates were delayed in swimming, meaning the area they swam after 6h and 8h was significantly 
lower compared to the ancestor, but they still reached the edge of the plate after 24h. However, it 
has to be noted that another form of motility, e.g. sliding, might mask the effect of swimming after 
24h. In addition, most of the isolates were either delayed, reduced or even impaired in their ability to 
swarm.  
When testing the isolates 1A_1, 1A_2, 1D_1 and 1D_2 for their ability to swim, the ancestor spread 
over 36.2% of the whole swimming-plate after 6h and 87.1% after 8h, while the whole plate was 
covered after 24h (Fig. 19). The SN isolate 1A_1, showed delayed swimming motility, covering only 5.2 
% of the plate after 6h and 12.4% after 8h, while the whole plate was covered after 24h (Fig. 19). The 
WR isolate 1A_2 exhibited delayed swimming motility, spreading only 7.1% and 9.9% of the after 6h 
and 8h, respectively, while the whole plate was covered after 24h (Fig. 19). The SM isolate 1D_1 
showed delayed swimming motility, covering only 0.9% of the plate after 6h and 1.6% after 8h, while 
the whole plate was covered after 24h (Fig. 19). The WR isolate 1D_2 exhibited delayed swimming 
motility, spreading 15.1% and 25.9% after 6h and 8h, respectively, while the whole plate was covered 
after 24h (Fig. 19).

2µl ON cultures, with an OD adjusted to 5.0, in the middle of a 0.3% LB-agar plate in triplicates. Plates were incubated at

5.3.5. Growth of evolved isolates 
Compounds, produced and secreted by the plants, acted as the major carbon source during the 
evolution experiment, as it is also the case in a natural soil environment. In order to investigate, if B. 
subtilis evolved to have improved utilization of plant-produced compounds, evolved isolates were 
tested for growth in a minimal medium supplemented with plant polysaccharides as carbon source 
(MSNc + 0.5% xylan) at 24°C. 
Overall, most of the evolved isolates showed altered growth in MSNc + xylan, as all isolates had either 
a higher growth rate, a higher maximum OD or showed a delayed or no decline compared to the 
ancestor.  
When testing growth of isolates evolved on A. thaliana in response to the plant polysaccharide xylan, 
the ancestor had a lag phase of around 17-18h, after which it proceeded to grow until it reached 
stationary phase with a rate of 0.038h-1 after around 26-27h at a maximum OD of 0.94 (Fig. 25). After 
around 32h the OD started to decline, reaching an OD of 0.67 at the end of the experiment (Fig. 25). 
In comparison to the ancestor, the two WR isolates 1A_2 and 1D_2 showed a reduced lag phase (14-
15h and 12-13h, respectively) and a higher growth rate of 0.057h-1 each reaching the stationary phase 
earlier after around 23-24h with a higher overall OD (1.03 and 1.06, respectively) (Fig. 25). They started 
to decline earlier, however not as rapid as the ancestor and they also kept a higher OD until the end 
Fig. 24: Swarming motility of evolved isolates (2B_2, 2B_3, 2C_1 and 2C_3) in comparison to the ancestor on 0.7% LB-agar. 
Swarming motility of evolved isolates (2B_2, 2B_3, 2C_1 and 2C_3) as well as of the ancestor was tested by spotting 2µl ON 
cultures, with an OD adjusted to 5.0, in the middle of a 0.7% LB-Agar plate in triplicates. Plates were incubated at 30°C and 
pictures were taken after 8h, 10h and 24h. The area covered by bacteria, as well as the whole area of the agar plate was 
measured using ImageJ. Boxes represent the proportion of the area of the agar plate, which was covered by the bacteria at 
the given time for the three replicates. 
==PAGE:59==
46 
 
of the experiment (0.88 and 0.84, respectively) (Fig. 25). The SN isolates 1A_1 and 1E_3 showed a 
reduced lag phase (both around 13-14h) but a lower growth rate of 0.028h-1 and 0.030h-1, respectively 
(Fig. 25). They seemed to continue growing slowly until the end of the experiment, even though not 
exponentially after around 26-27h reaching a final OD of 1.07 and 1.05, respectively (Fig. 25). A similar 
lag phase compared to the ancestor was observed for the SM isolate 1D_1 (16-17h), while the other 
SM isolate 1E_2 showed a slightly reduced lag phase (14-15h ) (Fig. 25). Both SM proceeded to grow 
with higher growth rates (0.050h-1 and 0.051h-1, respectively), reaching stationary phase after around 
27-28h with a higher maximum OD (both 1.06), compared to the ancestor. After around 34-35 both 
1D_1 and 1E_2 declined reaching a final OD of 0.81 and 0.76, respectively (Fig. 25). The WRS isolate 
1E_3 showed a slightly reduced lag phase of 15-16h and proceeded to grow with a faster rate of 
0.046h-1, reaching stationary phase after around 23-24h, with, compared to the ancestor, a similar 
maximum OD of 0.95 (Fig. 25). Its stationary phase was reduced, entering a faster decline already after 
27-28h and reaching a final OD of 0.68 (Fig. 25).

When testing growth of isolates evolved on Tomato in response to the plant polysaccharide xylan, the 
ancestor had a lag phase of around 17-18h, after which it proceeded to grow until it reached stationary 
phase with a rate of 0.038h-1  after around 26-27h at a maximum OD of 0.94 (Fig. 26). After around 
32h the OD started to decline, reaching an OD of 0.67 (Fig. 26). In comparison to the ancestor, the 
WRS isolates 2A_1 and 2B_2 showed a slightly reduced lag phase of around 14-15h and proceeded to 
grow with a higher growth rate of 0.047h-1 and 0.057h-1, respectively, reaching stationary phase after

a higher growth rate of 0.063h-1 and but with a higher maximum OD of 1.03, compared to the ancestor 
(Fig. 26). It started to decline earlier (after 29-30h) but kept a higher OD until the end of the 
experiment reaching a final value of 0.79 (Fig. 26). The WR isolate 2C_3 showed a similar lag phase as 
the ancestor (17-18h) and also reached stationary after around 26-27h, with a higher growth rate of 
0.046h-1 (Fig. 26). It reached a slightly higher maximum OD of 0.96 and started to decline around the 
same time as the ancestor (after 32h), however not as rapid reaching a final OD of 0.81 (Fig. 26).

5.4. 
Competition between isolates evolved on distinct plant species 
In order to further investigate, whether B. subtilis adapted specifically in root colonization towards 
the plant species it evolved on, an isolate that evolved on A. thaliana (1E_3) was tested in direct 
competition in root colonization against an isolate that evolved on Tomato (2A_1). Therefore, both 
14-16d old A. thaliana, as well as 8-10d old Tomato seedlings, were inoculated with a 1:1 mix of 
contrary red- and green-fluorescent-labeled isolates and incubated for 48h. Afterwards, the plant 
roots were vortexed to disrupt the biofilm and the suspension was plated on LB-agar to allow for CFU 
counting. As a control, both isolates were also tested in competition against the ancestor. 
No clear trend was observed in either of the competitions, as in none of competitions, the ratio 
changed significantly compared to the inoculation ratio of 1:1 (Fig. 27). In the competition between 
the isolate 1E_3 and 2A_1 the final proportions on A. thaliana were 43.0% ± 2.7% and 57.0% ± 2.7%, 
respectively, while the final ratio on Tomato came to 58.0% ± 5.3% against 42.0% ± 5.3%, respectively 
(Fig. 27). In direct competition against the ancestor, the evolved isolate 1E_3 made up 61.5% ± 3.7% 
of the final community on A. thaliana, while its final proportion on Tomato came to 57.9% ± 5.3% (Fig.

ON culture to 0.1 in MSNC +0.5% xylan and transferring 100µl into each well of a 96-microtiter well plate.

5.5. 
Root colonization of mixed populations 
In order to investigate, whether and how different morphology types interact when colonizing a plant 
root together, 14-16d old A. thaliana seedlings were inoculated with a 1:1:1 mixture of one SN (1E_1) 
isolate, one SM(1E_3) isolate and either one WRS isolate (1E_3), one WR isolate(1E_4, not described 
previously, Morphology in Supp. 7), which have all evolved on A. thaliana, or the ancestor. After 48h 
of incubation in a plant chamber, roots were vortexed to disrupt the biofilm and the suspension was 
plated on LB-agar to allow for CFU counting and identification of morphotypes. The observed total 
root colonization of the community was compared to a calculated prediction, which for each isolate 
was calculated as the product of the proportion of the isolate in the initial inoculation mix and its 
performance in mono colonization.  
When colonizing alone, the SN isolate as well as the WRS isolate showed significantly improved root 
colonization compared to the ancestor, while the SM isolate showed significantly reduced root 
colonization. No significant change in root colonization was observed for the WR isolate (Fig. 28).   
Fig. 27: Competition in root colonization between an isolate evolved on A. thaliana (1E_3) and an isolate evolved 
on Tomato (2A_1) as well as each isolate against the ancestor on both A. thaliana and Tomato. Both 14-16d old A. 
thaliana (blank) and 8-10d old Tomato seedlings (shaded) were inoculated with a 1:1 mix of an isolate evolved only 
on A. thaliana (1E_3) and an isolate evolved only on Tomato, as well as a 1:1 mix of each isolate and the ancestor and 
incubated for 48h in a plant chamber. The 1:1 mix was plated on LB-agar to determine the ratio of the two strains in 
the initial inoculum. The biofilm on the roots was disrupted by vortexing with small glass beads and the suspension 
was plated on LB-agar to allow CFU counting. The number of cells colonizing the roots was normalized for each isolate 
by dividing with its starting CFU in the 1:1 mix. The ratio of colonization capacity was calculated by dividing the 
normalized CFU of each isolate of the 1:1 mix with the sum of both normalized CFU values. 
==PAGE:63==
50 
 
All three mixes showed similar patterns when colonizing the root, meaning that the observed number 
of total cells was higher than the predicted value. The SM as well as WRS, WR and the Ancestor 
performed better in the mix than predicted based on their mono colonization.  
The mixed community with the SN, SM and WRS isolates showed only a minor increase (~1.2-fold) in 
root colonization compared to the predicted value (Fig. 28). However, in this mix the, SM isolate 
performed approx. 7.0 times better than predicted, while the WRS isolate increased slightly (~1.2-
fold) (Fig. 28). On the contrary, the SN isolate was reduced in root colonization (~1.7-fold), compared 
to its predicted value (Fig. 28). 
Compared to its predicted value, the total mixed community with the SN, SM and WR isolates showed 
an approx. 2.2-fold increase in root colonization (Fig. 28). In this mix, the SM isolate performed approx. 
3.6 times better than predicted, while the WR isolate increased approx. 8.0-fold (Fig. 28). In contrast, 
the SN isolate was reduced approx. 2.4-fold in root colonization compared to its predicted value (Fig. 
28).

The mixed community with the SN, SM isolates and the ancestor showed the highest increase (~3.8-
fold) in total root colonization compared to its predicted value (Fig. 28). In this mix, the SM isolate 
performed approx. 16.7 times better than predicted, while the ancestor increased approx. 21.6-fold

Furthermore, two additional biological replicates were conducted for the mixed community with the 
WRS isolate, while one additional biological replicate was performed for the community with the WR 
isolate, as well as the community with the ancestor. All of the biological replicates were conducted at 
different time points. For the combination of SN:SM:WR and SN:SM:Anc the same pattern was 
observed, meaning that the total observed number of cells, as well as the number of WR, SM and 
ancestor cells in the mixed colonization is higher than the predicted value (Supp. 4). The biological 
replicates of the SN:SM:WRS mix seemed to vary, as one showed no increase in overall CFU compared 
to the predicted value, while the other showed even a slight decrease (Supp. 5,6). Still, in both 
replicates the SM variant was improved in mixed colonization compared to its mono colonization 
(Supp. 5,6).
