6. Discussion  
PGPR, such as B. subtilis, are a promising eco-friendly alternative to traditionally used agrochemicals, 
as they harbor many important plant growth-promoting as well as biocontrol properties (Etesami & 
Alikhani, 2018; Lucy et al. 2004). Even though their potential seems immense under laboratory 
conditions and in greenhouse trials, their success varies greatly when applied in fields (Bardin et al. 
2015; Maplestone & Campbell 1989; Moreira & de Mio 2015; Xu et al. 2011). Understanding the 
underlying mechanisms of how B. subtilis interacts with and colonizes the plants will, therefore, 
contribute to improve its application. The main objective of this thesis was to investigate, how B. 
subtilis evolves during root colonization on two distinct plant species, namely A. thaliana and Tomato, 
and what role key bacterial properties such as motility and biofilm formation play in root (re-) 
colonization. Furthermore, we were interested in testing whether B. subtilis adapts specifically 
towards the plant species it is evolved on, or whether it exhibits general adaptation to plants. This 
part of the thesis discusses the obtained results from the evolution experiments and compares the 
major findings to other evolutionary studies.  
6.1. 
Rapid adaptation and diversification of evolved populations 
B. subtilis improved rapidly in root colonization in all four evolution experiments, independent of the 
plant species it evolved on, as well as whether it evolved only on one or alternating between the two 
plant species (Fig. 8). It has to be noted, that the number of cells colonizing the root was not measured 
directly after initial colonization (48h), but after re-colonization of the subsequent seedling in the 
evolution experiment (96h). For this, not only the ability to colonize the plant successfully but also 
persistence on the root is of importance. Nevertheless, across all four evolution experiments, the 
number of cells on the plant root increased approx. 10-fold or more already after the fifth transfer, 
while no significant change in CFU was observed afterwards.  
As shown by Lenski and colleagues in an inspiring manner in the long-term E. coli evolution 
experiment, fitness often increases rapidly and repeatable in the beginning of the evolution 
experiment, while it slows down over time (Lenski et al. 1991, Lenski & Travisano 1994). Rapid 
adaptation might be especially applicable in experimental evolution of biofilm populations, as the 
complex biofilm matrix gives rise to distinct gradients, e.g. in oxygen and nutrients (De Beer et al. 
1994, Stewart & Franklin, 2008). Indeed, the presence of gradients often leads to stresses, such as 
oxidative stress, which induces mutagenesis and leads to an increased mutation rate in biofilm 
populations (Ryder et al. 2012; Saint-Ruf et al. 2014). Often loss-of-function mutations of otherwise 
costly properties, such as unused catabolic functions, occur early on in the experiment, leading to 
novel genotypes, better adapted to the experimental conditions (Cooper & Lenski, 2000). The results 
from re-sequenced evolved isolates might provide clues, whether such loss-of-function mutations also 
==PAGE:66==
53 
 
occurred in the present evolution experiments and whether they might have contributed to the rapid 
improvement in root colonization. 
Furthermore, gradients combined with the spatial structure prevalent in biofilm communities provide 
vacant niches, which might not be utilized by the initially existing genotype and are therefore 
accessible to novel genotypes (Yoder et al. 2010). As a result of this, monocultures of an initially 
identical clonal lineage often undergo rapid diversification in experimental evolution in biofilm 
populations, leading to the coexistence of distinct morphotypes (Korona et al. 1994; Poltak & Cooper, 
2011; McDonald et al. 2009; Rainey & Travisano, 1998). Here we observed, next to the WR variant, 
which is indistinguishable from the ancestor, a distinct SN variant already after five transfers in all 
lineages across all evolution experiments (Fig. 9B). Furthermore, after ten transfers, an additional SM 
variant was observed in more than half of the lineages across all assays (Fig. 9C). The three distinct 
morphotypes coexisted until the end of the evolution experiment, reaching on average highly similar 
ratios of approx. 80% WR, 17% SN and 3% SM in all four evolution experiments (Fig. 10), illustrating 
the astounding degree of parallelism often observed in evolution experiments. Indeed, the 
diversification of the population is one of the key findings commonly occurring in many biofilm 
evolution studies (Dragoš et al. 2018a; Poltak & Cooper, 2011; Rainey & Travisano, 1998). Poltak & 
Cooper (2011), for example, selected, similar to our study, for a regular cycle of dispersal and biofilm 
formation, by letting Burkholderia cenocepacia colonize a plastic bead for 24h under shaken 
conditions and continuously transferring the colonized bead to a new sterile one. They also observed 
a rapid diversification of the founding clonal population into three distinct morphotypes, with the first, 
a Studded variant, occurring already after ~150 generations, while the other two, a Ruffled variant 
and a Wrinkly variant, appeared after ~300 generations (Poltak & Cooper, 2011). The three distinct 
morphotypes coexisted for more than 1000 generations and evened out at a ratio of ~88% Studded, 
9% Ruffled and 3% Wrinkly (Poltak & Cooper, 2011).  
Even though, as the name already suggests, classification into distinct morphotypes is mostly based 
on the morphological appearance of their colonies, they can also vary greatly in other phenotypic 
characteristics, including growth dynamics, biofilm formation, swimming or swarming motility and 
dispersal (Boles et al. 2004; Penterman et al. 2014; Poltak & Cooper, 2011; Rainey & Travisano, 1998; 
Steenackers et al. 2016). Because improvements in many of these properties, especially biofilm 
formation and motility, might be highly advantageous for root colonization, the diversification into 
specialized morphotypes might be the cause of the observed rapid improvement in root colonization 
of evolved populations. However, it has to be noted that even isolates from a given morphotype, 
which are indistinguishable from one another in colony morphology, can also often differ in certain 
phenotypic traits, because they might harbor additional mutations or because different mutations 
==PAGE:67==
54 
 
might yield the same morphotype (Boles et al. 2004; Kirisits et al. 2005; Steenackers et al. 2016). 
Therefore, randomly selected evolved isolates were tested for improvements in root colonization, 
biofilm formation, swimming and swarming motility as well as growth in response to plant 
polysaccharides.  
6.2. 
Improvements of evolved isolates 
Overall, only half of the evolved isolates showed significantly improved root colonization on either of 
the plant species, while one isolate showed even reduced root colonization on both plants. 
Interestingly, the proportion of isolates which improved in root colonization seemed to depend 
thereby on the plant species of the evolution experiment, as eight out of twelve isolates evolved on 
A. thaliana showed enhanced root colonization (Fig. 14), while it was only three out of nine isolates 
evolved on Tomato (Fig. 15). Changes in root colonization did not necessarily correlate with the 
distinct morphotypes. Even though all tested SN isolates were improved in root colonization, it varied 
for SM and WR. In order to test whether improved root colonization was associated with enhanced 
motility or biofilm formation, additional phenotypic tests were performed.   
6.2.1. Motility 
During the initial phase of root colonization, as well as during recolonization, bacterial cells have to 
actively move towards the plant roots to be able to establish themselves on the root (Allard-
Massicotte et al. 2016). In a static liquid medium, swimming is the primary mode of bacterial motility 
and it might, therefore, be an essential trait for successful root (re-)colonization in our system. In this 
thesis, it was demonstrated that indeed, a non-motile ∆hag mutant is outcompeted by the WT in 
direct competition in root (re-)colonization on A. thaliana in a static system, where active movement 
is required for cells to reach the root (Fig. 6). On the contrary, this effect is lost under shaken 
conditions, where the impact of motility is reduced, and the final ratio of WT to ∆hag mutant cells on 
the plant root is nearly 1:1. This illustrates the importance of motility for root (re-)colonization in a 
static system in liquid media, as was the case in the conducted evolution experiments. In addition, 
chemotaxis has been proposed to be required during the initial phase of root colonization, as it allows 
bacteria to detect secreted root exudates and move accordingly along the gradient towards the root 
(Allard-Massicotte et al. 2016).  
Here we tested swimming motility and the chemotactic potential of evolved isolates on 0.3% LB-agar 
plates, on which cells that grow in the center deplete nutrients and thereby give rise to a gradient, 
which leads to a chemotactically driven migration towards the edge of the plate (Kearns & Losick, 
2003). Surprisingly, none of the evolved isolates showed indications of improvements in chemotaxis 
or swimming motility in general. On the contrary, most of the tested isolates showed reduced 
swimming after 6h and 8h hours compared to the ancestral strain (Fig. 19-21). Even though all of the 
isolates reached the edge of the plate eventually after 24h, it cannot be excluded that at least some 
==PAGE:68==
55 
 
of the strains are highly impaired in swimming and chemotaxis, as a different mode of motility might 
mask the impact of swimming after 24h. Sliding, for example, is a passive form of movement that 
requires no active motor but is based on the pushing force of dividing cells and the production of 
additional components, which promote the expansion over surfaces, e.g. surfactants (Henrichsen, 
1972; Kearns, 2010). To exclude the effect of other modes of motility, changes in the chemotactic 
response were additionally tested in capillary assays containing extracted root exudates. 
Unfortunately, this assay showed great variation and was inconclusive, because all isolates as well as 
the ancestor showed a similar response towards the negative water control compared to the root 
exudates (Supp. 21). This method needs therefore to be optimized and repeated, in order to be able 
to validate changes in the chemotactic response of evolved isolates towards the root exudates of the 
plant species they evolved on.  
In addition to swimming, swarming motility, a multicellular and active form of movement across semi-
solid surfaces, has been proposed to be essential for root colonization (Gao et al. 2016). In our study, 
none of the evolved isolates improved in swarming (Fig. 22-24). On the contrary, all of the tested 
evolved isolates showed delayed, reduced or completely impaired swarming motility on 0.7% LB-agar. 
In addition, changes in swarming motility seemed to correlate with the distinct morphotypes, as both 
of the tested SN variants accounted for two of the three isolates able to reach the edge after 24h, 
while the two tested SM variants were the only isolates completely impaired in swarming. It has to be 
noted that the evolution experiment was conducted in liquid medium, in which swarming might be 
non-essential. A loss of the ability to swarm might therefore even improve competitive fitness since 
an otherwise costly but redundant function is lost.  
In addition, that none of the evolved isolates showed improvements in either swimming or swarming 
might be caused by antagonistic pleiotropy or an evolutionary trade-off between biofilm formation 
and motility, as they are known to be oppositely regulated (Blair et al. 2008). Therefore, mutations 
leading to enhanced biofilm formation might cause reduced motility as a side effect (Taylor & Buckling, 
2011). Van Ditmarsch et al. (2013) observed a trend where good swarmers tended to be poor biofilm 
formers, while good biofilm formers tended to be poor swarmers when testing 29 clinical and 18 
environmental Pseudomonas aeruginosa isolates. In this thesis, a similar trend was observed, as most 
of the isolates, apart from SM, showed more wrinkled pellicles, indicating enhanced biofilm formation 
(Fig. 16, 17), but were highly reduced in motility. Lastly, improvements in motility do not seem to 
necessarily correlate with improvements in root colonization, as all of the tested isolates, even the 
once enhanced in root colonization,  were reduced or at least delayed in motility.  
==PAGE:69==
56 
 
6.2.2. Biofilm formation 
PGPR, such as B. subtilis, are known to colonize plant roots in the form of biofilms, and mutants 
depleted in their ability to form a stable biofilm often fail to establish themselves on the plant or 
exhibit reduced colonization (Chen et al. 2013, Dragoš et al. 2018b). Therefore, improvements in 
biofilm formation might correlate with improved root colonization (Chen et al. 2013). Furthermore, 
experimental evolution selecting for biofilm formation, often observe rapid improvement in biofilm 
formation of evolved strains (Dragoš et al. 2018a; Poltak & Cooper, 2011).  
Here, we observed that most of the tested isolates evolved during root colonization, showed changes 
in biofilm structure in response to the plant polysaccharide xylan, as observed by the complexity of 
pellicles under the stereomicroscope (Fig. 16,17). These changes seem to strongly correlate with the 
distinct colony morphologies. All tested SN variants showed thereby very thick pellicles (Fig. 16), with 
a significantly increased biomass, as seen in fresh and dry weight of pellicles (Fig. 11). On the contrary, 
SM variants were not able to form a stable pellicle at all, but just a thin layer at the air-liquid interface. 
Most of the WR variants exhibited a slightly more structured pellicle, even though no significant 
difference in biomass was observed. Interestingly, none of the tested isolates showed a significantly 
improved pellicle productivity in the sense of an increase in the number of cells forming the pellicle 
(Fig. 18). On the contrary, three isolates were even significantly reduced in pellicle productivity. 
Furthermore, the SM variants forming thin pellicles, which could not be harvested for CFU 
quantification may also be reduced in pellicle productivity. However, it has to be noted that, even 
though they share most of the regulatory pathways, there are differences in pellicle and biofilm 
formation on a solid surface, as it is the case on plant roots (Terra et al., 2012). Therefore, a decrease 
in pellicle CFU might not necessarily correspond to a decrease in biofilm productivity on plant roots. 
To confirm that improvements of CFU correlate with enhanced biofilm formation on plant roots, 
colonization by fluorescent-labeled isolates could be examined directly under the fluorescence 
microscope.  
Overall, pellicle formation and plant root colonization might correlate at least for isolates evolved on 
and colonizing A. thaliana. First of all, the only two WR isolates evolved on A. thaliana, which showed 
significantly decreased pellicle productivity, were also the only two WR isolates, which did not improve 
in root colonization on A. thaliana. On the other side, even though both SM isolates showed highly 
reduced pellicle formation, only one of them showed significantly reduced root colonization. 
Furthermore, even though both SN isolates showed a higher number of cells colonizing the root and 
enhanced biofilm biomass, they did not exhibit improved pellicle CFU. Therefore, to confirm the 
correlation between pellicle formation and root colonization, more isolates, especially of the SM 
variant, need to be tested.  
==PAGE:70==
57 
 
6.2.3. Growth 
In an otherwise nutrient-poor soil, PGPR depend on nutrients provided by plants through the secretion 
of root exudates (Beauregard, 2015). This was mimicked in the evolutionary system, by using a 
minimal salt medium and thereby stimulate bacterial cells to colonize plants in order to utilize 
resources efficiently. It has previously been observed in E. coli, that evolving populations specialize 
their diet rapidly in order to maximize their fitness in the present environment (Cooper & Lenski, 
2000).  
When testing evolved isolates for changes in growth in response to the plant polysaccharide xylan, we 
observed that most of the isolates improved, either by growing faster and to a higher OD or by 
prolonging the stationary phase (Fig. 25, 26). Furthermore, changes in growth correlated with distinct 
morphotypes. Both SN isolates did not enter a decline but seemed to proceed growing slowly until the 
end of the experiment, indicating that cells might remain vital for a longer period of time and delay 
sporulation. However, it has to be noted, that during the growth assay, the SN isolates tended to 
aggregate when reaching high cell density and clumps may have impacted the measurements. To 
reduce this effect, at each time point, nine points of each well were measured. Nevertheless, to 
further validate, whether SN isolates are delayed in sporulation, specific sporulation assays have to be 
carried out. Both SM isolates showed faster growth, a higher maximum OD and a prolonged stationary 
phase, compared to the ancestor, even though they eventually declined, unlike SN. However, from 
this data alone it is impossible to distinguish, if improvements in growth are caused by a possibly 
enhanced xylan utilization and/or specialization in diet or if it is a by-product of the loss of biofilm 
formation and motility capabilities, as this might lower the cost for SM and thus lead to faster growth. 
The WR isolates varied also in growth, some showing improvements while others did not. Remarkably, 
one WRS isolate, 1E_3, entered the cell death phase noticeably earlier than the ancestor. This seems 
to be contradicting with its root colonization potential because it showed a significant increase in the 
number of cells on A. thaliana roots. Since xylan is just one of the carbon sources provided by the 
plants, isolate 1E_3 might have specialized on another plant polysaccharide, to evade competition 
with other isolates. It should, therefore, be considered for further tests to identify, what might cause 
the rapid decline in planktonic culture of this isolate, and if this decline is also observed in other growth 
media.  
6.3. 
Specificity in root colonization  
Variation in the composition of the root microbiome from plants occurring in the same environment 
seems to correlate with the host species, and bacteria isolated from a specific species have been 
shown to prefer to colonize their original host over others (Fitzpatrick et al. 2017; Zhang et al. 2014). 
One possible factor contributing to this might be the fact that distinct plant species also produce and 
secrete distinct root exudates (Warembourg et al. 2003). These act not only as major carbon source 
==PAGE:71==
58 
 
for PGPR in an otherwise nutrient-depleted soil and as an inducer of biofilm formation, but also as 
chemical signal and initiator of root colonization via bacterial chemotaxis (Beauregard et al. 2012; 
Beauregard, 2015). Therefore, bacteria might evolve specifically to colonize a given host plant, by 
adapting their diet and/or chemotactic response towards certain components present in root 
exudates. Furthermore, differences in the sheer physiological architecture of plant roots, including 
size, thickness, abundance of lateral roots and root hairs, might provide even more variation in 
environmental conditions between distinct plant species (Saleem et al. 2018).  
To identify if evolved isolates show plant species-specific adaptation paths, or if they improved 
generally in root colonization on both plants, we tested isolates evolved on either A. thaliana or on 
Tomato for root colonization on both plant species in mono colonization as well as in direct 
competition. We observed indications of specific adaptation towards a certain plant species, at least 
in some of the evolved isolates. Most of the isolates evolved on A. thaliana improved in root 
colonization only on A. thaliana, but not on Tomato (Fig. 14). In contrast, only one of the isolates 
evolved on Tomato also improved in root colonization on Tomato, while two isolates improved only 
on A. Thaliana (Fig. 15). However, it has to be noted, that those three isolates tended towards 
improved root colonization on the other plant species as well. Obviously, general adaptations 
improving B. subtilis in root colonization, such as a general enhanced matrix production or motility, 
might be selected for in either evolution experiment, independent of the plant species. Furthermore, 
because Tomato seedlings used in this experiment are considerably bigger in size compared to A. 
thaliana seedlings, they provide a larger surface area to colonize, as well as a higher amount of 
nutrients through root exudates. Therefore, potential general adaptations to plant roots arising during 
evolution on A. thaliana, which provide an advantage to the evolved when tested on A. thaliana, may 
not be observed when tested on Tomato, as this plant provides plenty of space and nutrients. From 
these results alone, it is difficult to fully distinguish, whether at least some isolates evolved specifically 
in root colonization towards one plant species, or if general adaptations in root colonization are not 
detected when testing evolved isolates for improved colonization on Tomato seedlings.  
To further validate the specificity of evolved isolates, we tested two isolates, one evolved on A. 
thaliana and another evolved on Tomato, in direct competition on either plant species. The isolates 
were selected based on their similarity in colony morphology and on their performance in root 
colonization in monoculture, with either showing improved colonization only on the plant species they 
evolved on. Because the final ratios on both plant species did not differ significantly from a ratio of 
1:1, improvements in root colonization might not be specific towards the plant species the isolates 
were evolved on (Fig. 27). However, it has to be noted, that also neither of the isolates was able to 
outcompete the ancestor in direct competition, even though they improved significantly in root 
==PAGE:72==
59 
 
colonization in monoculture, compared to the ancestor. Testing only one isolate of each evolution 
experiment might give some indications, however, they are obviously not representative enough and 
more isolates have to be tested. Furthermore, if the competitive edge one isolate has over the other 
is only minor, it might not show already after the initial root colonization, but might need some time, 
i.e. re-colonization, to do so. Therefore, isolates should also be tested in direct competition for root 
re-colonization for a few transfers. In addition, to be able to fully validate, whether B. subtilis adapted 
specifically towards one of the two plant species, one might look at responses in specific bacterial 
properties, such as biofilm formation, chemotaxis and growth, in response to root exudates directly 
extracted from each plant species as done by Zhang et al. (2014).  
6.4. 
Root colonization of mixed communities 
When testing our evolved isolates for enhanced root colonization on the plant they evolved on, we 
observed a considerable number of isolates that did not show any improvements, while one isolate 
was even significantly reduced. It is surprising, that such an isolate still managed to persist in the 
evolved populations and was not outcompeted and selected against in the course of evolution. 
However, a key finding of experimental evolution in biofilm models is, that the productivity of evolved 
populations is not necessarily just the sum of the productivity of its members in monocultures, as 
interactions between distinct evolved variants might occur (Brockhurst et al., 2006; Ellis et al. 2015; 
Poltak & Cooper, 2011). First of all, trough diversification of the population, competition might be 
evaded, as distinct variants specialize in occupying different niches (Yoder et al. 2010). Furthermore, 
diversification might also provide new niches as one morphotype might alter environmental 
conditions in which novel specialists are able to thrive in (Day et al. 2003). For example, Poltak & 
Cooper (2011) observed in their bead transfer model with B. cenocepacia that distinct colony variants 
inhabited distinct niches in the system, in which one variant inhabited a unique layer on top of the 
biofilm formed by others. Intriguingly, they also showed that cross-feeding interactions evolved, in 
which distinct morphotypes depended on by-products of each other. Indeed, the evolution of 
cooperative traits, which promotes productivity by redistributing resources more efficiently, has been 
commonly observed in experimental evolution of biofilm models. Contrary to planktonic cultures, the 
structured environment in biofilm communities might stabilize cooperation, because public good 
producers might retain costly but beneficial components, e.g. iron scavenging siderophores, more 
efficiently than in a homogeneous, well-mixed environment. However, as seen in the experimental 
evolution of B. subtilis pellicles, conducted by Dragoš et al. (2018a), in which one of the morphotypes 
lost its ability to form a biofilm in monoculture,  but in co-culture, this morphotype exploited matrix 
production of co-occurring variants, biofilm communities are still prone to cheats and exploitative 
interactions might evolve.  
==PAGE:73==
60 
 
In this thesis,  A. thaliana roots were inoculated with a 1:1:1 mix of the distinct morphotypes SN, SM 
and WR which resulted in an overall higher root colonization than its predicted value based on the 
performance of the community members in mono colonization (Fig. 28). However, not every 
community member benefited from the presence of the others, but only SM and WR tended to 
improve, while SN was decreased in two out of three different mixed populations, compared to the 
predicted values. As seen in pellicle assays, the SN variant seems to builds a thicker, more complex 
structured pellicle, indicating an enhanced matrix production. The overproduction of matrix 
represents an increase in cost for this isolate, resulting in more energy being used for matrix 
production instead of growth (Dragoš et al. 2018b). A decrease in the productivity of SN compared to 
the expected value in the mixed root colonization might, therefore, be caused by an overproduction 
of matrix components, from which other co-occurring morphotypes can benefit without having the 
cost of the production themselves. Especially the SM variant, which seems to have lost its ability to 
form a stable biofilm on its own, might exploit this overproduction of matrix components by the SN 
variant, similar to what was observed in Dragoš et al. (2018a). Because in all lineages the SM variant 
has been observed only after SN has evolved, its rise and persistence in evolved populations might be 
dependent on the early occurrence of the overproducing SN variant. However, it has to be noted that 
the SM variant might already be present at an earlier time point, but only in succinct relative 
abundance, which makes it impossible to detect solely based on CFU, while it rises only to detectable 
level once SN has established itself. In accordance with this, re-sequencing of whole populations at 
different time-points might give an even more detailed view of the development of the populations 
during the evolution experiment. To further validate how SM is able to exploit the community and to 
integrate into the biofilm, the spatial distribution of distinct morphotypes can be tested in mixed root 
colonization of fluorescent-labeled isolates using confocal microscopy. Indeed, analyzing the spatial 
distribution of distinct morphotypes on plant roots enables to uncover how they position themselves 
and how they mix on the plant, providing thereby further clues how they interact on the root. 
Furthermore, to confirm that SN indeed overproduces matrix component(s) and identify which 
component(s), reporter genes should be tested to measure gene expression of known B. subtilis 
matrix genes.  
Even though all tested WR variants, including the ancestor, improved in co-colonization compared to 
their predicted value overall (Supp. 4), their performance varied in absolute numbers depending on 
the given isolate. This indicates that some WR might harbor additional mutations, especially the WRS 
subtype, which not only impact their individual performance, but also the interaction with other 
morphotypes.  
==PAGE:74==
61 
 
To get back to the research questions formulated in the beginning of this thesis (see 2. Research aims), 
you might answer them based on the presented results with:  
1. Yes, motility seems to be essential for successful root colonization in liquid media, as an a-
motile Δhag is outcompeted by the motile WT. Nevertheless, evolutionary constraints, e.g. in 
the form of a trade-off between biofilm formation and motility, might still select for decreased 
capacity in motility during evolution on plant roots. 
2. Yes, B. subtilis rapidly improves in root colonization during experimental evolution. However, 
in order to do so, B. subtilis does not necessarily also improve in biofilm formation or motility 
but distinct adaptation strategies are observed.  
3. Yes and no, as both, a specialist and generalist strategy, seems to be possible. Clear indications 
were observed for the evolution on A. thaliana were eight out of twelve isolates showed plant-
specific adaptation towards A. thaliana. However, this was not the case for the evolution on 
Tomato, where only one isolate showed Tomato-specific adaptation, while two isolates 
seemed to be only improved on A. thaliana.  
4. Yes, populations diversify quickly when evolving on plant roots and distinct morphological 
variants co-existed until the end of the evolution. Indeed, this diversification seems to be 
highly beneficial for the evolved population, because the productivity of a mixed community 
is enhanced, compared to the predicted value based on the individual performance of each 
member in monoculture. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:75==
62 
 
6.5. 
Future outlook for the application of B. subtilis 
Here we observed for the first time diversification of a population of initially identical clones and 
evolution of exploitative interactions on plant roots. This delivers important insights in plant root 
colonization, which have to be recognized for the application of B. subtilis as a biocontrol strain. First 
of all, biofilm formation is widely recognized to be an essential trait for PGPR to be able to successfully 
colonize plant roots and enhanced biofilm formation has been thought to correlate with 
improvements in root colonization (Chen et al. 2013). However, it seems to be not as simple as this. 
Even though it might apply for bacterial strains colonizing in monoculture, it is not necessarily the ideal 
strategy in a natural system, in which plants harbor a diverse microbiome. Indeed, the interaction of 
applied PGPR, such as B. subtilis, with the natural occurring microbiome has to be addressed urgently, 
as until now, only very little is known (Qiao et al. 2017; Wei et al. 2016). It is of special importance, to 
understand how B. subtilis integrates into the natural community on plant roots. Here we observed 
that evolved isolates with a potentially enhanced matrix production, are restrained in mixed 
colonization, as they are exploited by co-colonizers, especially those which lost their ability to form a 
biofilm independently. On the contrary, genetic diversification might be a stable strategy to increase 
plant colonization. It needs to be tested whether this holds true in mixed-species biofilms and if 
enhanced matrix production might also be exploited by other bacterial species, or whether B. subtilis 
is more resistant towards an invasion of other bacterial species and indeed enhanced biofilm 
formation might prove advantageous in a mixed-species community.  
Secondly, as in most studies analyzing the importance of distinct bacterial properties for root 
colonization, all our experiments were conducted in liquid media. Obviously, this is far from a natural 
soil environment. It has therefore to be addressed what role bacterial properties, such as biofilm 
formation and especially swimming motility, play in a soil system.  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:76==
63 
 
7. Conclusion  
This thesis presents a first glimpse into experimental evolution of the PGPR B. subtilis on plant roots. 
Here we demonstrate, that B. subtilis populations improve rapidly in root colonization and the 
population diversifies into distinct morphological variants, independently of the plant species it 
evolved on. Already after five transfers, the number of cells colonizing the plant root has increased for 
~10-fold or more, across all four different evolutionary experiments. Correspondingly, a novel 
morphotype, termed SN, which was distinct from the original WR variant, was observed after five 
transfers and proved to be improved in pellicle biofilm formation in response to xylan and root 
colonization. Furthermore, after transfer ten, a third morphotype termed SM, co-existed in more than 
half of the evolutionary lineages. Contrary to the SN variant, SM showed unchanged or reduced root 
colonization potential and was correspondingly seemingly impaired in pellicle biofilm formation. 
Intriguingly, it still managed to persist in evolved populations until the end of the evolution 
experiment. Mixed colonization assays of the three distinct morphotypes revealed, that SM exploits 
co-colonizing morphotypes, especially SN, as its own colonization potential improves in co-
colonization, while at the same time the colonization potential of SN decreases. Therefore, even if it 
holds true in mono colonization, improvements in biofilm formation do not necessarily correspond to 
improvements in root colonization in a mixed system.  
Furthermore, even though isolates evolved on distinct plant species were not able to outcompete 
each other in direct competition on the plant species they evolved on, indications that B. subtilis might 
adapt indeed specifically in root colonization towards distinct plant species were observed. Here it 
was demonstrated that eight out of twelve isolates evolved on A. thaliana showed improved root 
colonization only on A. thaliana, but not on Tomato, while one was even additionally reduced on 
Tomato. 
In conclusion, this thesis especially highlights genetic diversification as a stable strategy to increase 
plant colonization and give indications for plant species-specific adaptation paths of B. subtilis.
